## Chapter: Exponents and order of operations

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Exponents and order of operations! In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of exponents and the order in which mathematical operations should be performed. These concepts are essential for mastering 6th-grade math and will serve as building blocks for more advanced mathematical topics.

Exponents are a shorthand way of representing repeated multiplication. They allow us to express numbers raised to a certain power, which can simplify complex calculations. Understanding how to work with exponents is crucial for solving equations, simplifying expressions, and evaluating numerical values.

Order of operations, also known as PEMDAS (Parentheses, Exponents, Multiplication and Division, Addition and Subtraction), is a set of rules that dictate the sequence in which mathematical operations should be performed. By following these rules, we ensure that calculations are done accurately and consistently.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into the rules and properties of exponents, including how to multiply and divide numbers with exponents, raise a power to a power, and simplify expressions with exponents. We will also explore the order of operations and learn how to apply it to solve mathematical problems involving multiple operations.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of exponents and the order of operations, enabling you to confidently tackle more complex mathematical problems. So let's dive in and master 6th-grade math!

### Section: Meaning of exponents

Exponents are a fundamental concept in mathematics that allow us to express numbers raised to a certain power. They provide a shorthand way of representing repeated multiplication. In this section, we will explore the meaning of exponents and how they can simplify complex calculations.

#### Understanding the concept of exponents

To understand exponents, let's start with a simple example. Consider the expression $2^3$. The number 2 is called the base, and the number 3 is called the exponent. The exponent tells us how many times the base should be multiplied by itself.

In this case, $2^3$ means multiplying 2 by itself three times: $2 \times 2 \times 2$. The result is 8. So, $2^3$ is equal to 8.

Exponents can also be negative or fractional. A negative exponent indicates that the base should be divided by itself raised to the positive exponent. For example, $2^{-2}$ is equal to $\frac{1}{2^2}$, which is $\frac{1}{4}$.

A fractional exponent represents taking the root of a number. For instance, $4^\frac{1}{2}$ is the square root of 4, which is 2.

Exponents have several important properties that allow us to simplify calculations. One of these properties is the product rule, which states that when multiplying two numbers with the same base, we can add their exponents. For example, $2^3 \times 2^2$ is equal to $2^{3+2}$, which simplifies to $2^5$.

Another property is the power rule, which states that when raising a power to another power, we can multiply the exponents. For instance, $(2^3)^2$ is equal to $2^{3 \times 2}$, which simplifies to $2^6$.

Understanding exponents is crucial for solving equations, simplifying expressions, and evaluating numerical values. They provide a powerful tool for working with numbers and can greatly simplify complex calculations.

In the next section, we will explore the rules and properties of exponents in more detail, including how to multiply and divide numbers with exponents, raise a power to a power, and simplify expressions with exponents. So let's continue our journey and delve deeper into the world of exponents!

### Section: Meaning of exponents

Exponents are a fundamental concept in mathematics that allow us to express numbers raised to a certain power. They provide a shorthand way of representing repeated multiplication. In this section, we will explore the meaning of exponents and how they can simplify complex calculations.

#### Understanding the concept of exponents

To understand exponents, let's start with a simple example. Consider the expression $2^3$. The number 2 is called the base, and the number 3 is called the exponent. The exponent tells us how many times the base should be multiplied by itself.

In this case, $2^3$ means multiplying 2 by itself three times: $2 \times 2 \times 2$. The result is 8. So, $2^3$ is equal to 8.

Exponents can also be negative or fractional. A negative exponent indicates that the base should be divided by itself raised to the positive exponent. For example, $2^{-2}$ is equal to $\frac{1}{2^2}$, which is $\frac{1}{4}$.

A fractional exponent represents taking the root of a number. For instance, $4^\frac{1}{2}$ is the square root of 4, which is 2.

Exponents have several important properties that allow us to simplify calculations. One of these properties is the product rule, which states that when multiplying two numbers with the same base, we can add their exponents. For example, $2^3 \times 2^2$ is equal to $2^{3+2}$, which simplifies to $2^5$.

Another property is the power rule, which states that when raising a power to another power, we can multiply the exponents. For instance, $(2^3)^2$ is equal to $2^{3 \times 2}$, which simplifies to $2^6$.

Understanding exponents is crucial for solving equations, simplifying expressions, and evaluating numerical values. They provide a powerful tool for working with numbers and can greatly simplify complex calculations.

#### Real-life examples of exponents

Exponents are not just abstract mathematical concepts; they have real-life applications as well. Let's explore some examples of how exponents are used in everyday situations:

1. **Compound interest**: When calculating compound interest, exponents come into play. The formula for compound interest is given by:

   $$A = P \left(1 + \frac{r}{n}\right)^{nt}$$

   In this formula, $A$ represents the final amount, $P$ is the principal amount, $r$ is the annual interest rate, $n$ is the number of times interest is compounded per year, and $t$ is the number of years. The exponent $nt$ represents the number of compounding periods over the given time.

2. **Population growth**: Exponents are used to model population growth. The exponential growth formula is given by:

   $$P(t) = P_0 \times (1 + r)^t$$

   Here, $P(t)$ represents the population at time $t$, $P_0$ is the initial population, $r$ is the growth rate, and $t$ is the time in years. The exponent $t$ represents the number of years the population has been growing.

3. **Scientific notation**: Exponents are used in scientific notation to express very large or very small numbers in a concise way. For example, the speed of light is approximately $3 \times 10^8$ meters per second. The exponent $8$ indicates that the decimal point should be moved 8 places to the right to obtain the actual value.

These are just a few examples of how exponents are used in real-life scenarios. Understanding exponents can help us make sense of the world around us and solve practical problems.

In the next section, we will explore the rules and properties of exponents in more detail, including how to multiply and divide numbers with exponents, raise a power to a power, and simplify expressions with exponents.

### Section: Powers of whole numbers

In the previous section, we learned about the meaning of exponents and how they can simplify complex calculations. Now, let's dive deeper into the topic and explore how to calculate powers of whole numbers.

#### Calculating powers of whole numbers

Calculating powers of whole numbers involves raising a number to a certain exponent. The exponent tells us how many times the base number should be multiplied by itself. Let's look at some examples to understand this concept better.

Example 1: $2^4$

In this example, the base number is 2, and the exponent is 4. To calculate this power, we need to multiply 2 by itself four times:

$2^4 = 2 \times 2 \times 2 \times 2$

Simplifying the multiplication, we get:

$2^4 = 16$

So, $2^4$ is equal to 16.

Example 2: $5^3$

Here, the base number is 5, and the exponent is 3. We need to multiply 5 by itself three times:

$5^3 = 5 \times 5 \times 5$

Simplifying the multiplication, we get:

$5^3 = 125$

So, $5^3$ is equal to 125.

Example 3: $10^2$

In this example, the base number is 10, and the exponent is 2. We need to multiply 10 by itself two times:

$10^2 = 10 \times 10$

Simplifying the multiplication, we get:

$10^2 = 100$

So, $10^2$ is equal to 100.

Calculating powers of whole numbers is a fundamental skill in mathematics. It allows us to express large numbers in a more concise and manageable way. By understanding how to calculate powers, we can solve equations, simplify expressions, and evaluate numerical values more efficiently.

Remember, when calculating powers, the base number is multiplied by itself as many times as the exponent indicates. Practice calculating powers of whole numbers to strengthen your understanding of this important concept.

### Section: Powers of whole numbers

#### Calculating powers of whole numbers

### Section: Powers of fractions and decimals

In the previous section, we learned about calculating powers of whole numbers. Now, let's extend our understanding to powers of fractions and decimals. 

#### Understanding powers of fractions and decimals

When it comes to fractions and decimals, the process of calculating powers is similar to that of whole numbers. However, there are a few additional considerations to keep in mind.

To calculate the power of a fraction or decimal, we raise the numerator and denominator (or the decimal value) to the given exponent separately. Let's look at some examples to understand this concept better.

Example 1: $\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^3$

In this example, we have a fraction with a numerator of 1 and a denominator of 2. The exponent is 3. To calculate this power, we need to raise both the numerator and denominator to the exponent:

$\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^3 = \left(1^3\right) \div \left(2^3\right)$

Simplifying the numerator and denominator, we get:

$\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^3 = \frac{1}{8}$

So, $\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^3$ is equal to $\frac{1}{8}$.

Example 2: $0.5^2$

Here, we have a decimal value of 0.5. The exponent is 2. To calculate this power, we need to raise 0.5 to the exponent:

$0.5^2 = 0.5 \times 0.5$

Simplifying the multiplication, we get:

$0.5^2 = 0.25$

So, $0.5^2$ is equal to 0.25.

Example 3: $\left(\frac{3}{4}\right)^2$

In this example, we have a fraction with a numerator of 3 and a denominator of 4. The exponent is 2. To calculate this power, we need to raise both the numerator and denominator to the exponent:

$\left(\frac{3}{4}\right)^2 = \left(3^2\right) \div \left(4^2\right)$

Simplifying the numerator and denominator, we get:

$\left(\frac{3}{4}\right)^2 = \frac{9}{16}$

So, $\left(\frac{3}{4}\right)^2$ is equal to $\frac{9}{16}$.

Calculating powers of fractions and decimals is an important skill in mathematics. It allows us to work with fractional and decimal values in a more efficient way. By understanding how to calculate these powers, we can solve equations, simplify expressions, and evaluate numerical values more effectively.

Now that we have learned about powers of fractions and decimals, let's practice our skills with some problems.

### Section: Powers of fractions and decimals

#### Understanding powers of fractions and decimals

### Section: Order of operations introduction

In the previous section, we learned about calculating powers of fractions and decimals. Now, let's move on to a new topic that is essential for understanding and solving mathematical expressions: the order of operations.

When we have a mathematical expression with multiple operations, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division, it is important to follow a specific order to ensure that we get the correct answer. This order is known as the order of operations.

The order of operations is a set of rules that tells us which operations to perform first, second, third, and so on. By following these rules, we can avoid any confusion or ambiguity in our calculations.

The order of operations can be remembered using the acronym PEMDAS, which stands for:

- P: Parentheses (or brackets)
- E: Exponents (or powers)
- MD: Multiplication and Division (from left to right)
- AS: Addition and Subtraction (from left to right)

Let's break down each of these rules and understand how they work.

#### Parentheses (or brackets)

The first rule of the order of operations is to perform any calculations inside parentheses or brackets first. This allows us to isolate and simplify parts of the expression before moving on to other operations.

For example, consider the expression: $2 \times (3 + 4)$. According to the order of operations, we need to calculate the sum inside the parentheses first, which gives us $2 \times 7$. Then, we can multiply 2 by 7 to get the final answer of 14.

#### Exponents (or powers)

The next rule is to evaluate any exponents or powers in the expression. An exponent tells us how many times a number should be multiplied by itself.

For example, in the expression $2^3$, the base number is 2 and the exponent is 3. This means that we need to multiply 2 by itself three times: $2 \times 2 \times 2$. The result is 8.

#### Multiplication and Division (from left to right)

After evaluating any exponents, we move on to the next rule, which is to perform any multiplication or division operations from left to right.

For example, consider the expression $6 \div 2 \times 3$. According to the order of operations, we need to divide 6 by 2 first, which gives us 3. Then, we multiply 3 by 3 to get the final answer of 9.

#### Addition and Subtraction (from left to right)

Finally, the last rule of the order of operations is to perform any addition or subtraction operations from left to right.

For example, in the expression $4 + 2 - 1$, we start by adding 4 and 2, which gives us 6. Then, we subtract 1 from 6 to get the final answer of 5.

By following the order of operations, we can ensure that our calculations are done correctly and consistently. This is especially important when dealing with complex expressions that involve multiple operations.

Now that we have a basic understanding of the order of operations, let's dive deeper into each rule and explore some examples to solidify our knowledge.

### Section: Order of operations introduction

In the previous section, we learned about calculating powers of fractions and decimals. Now, let's move on to a new topic that is essential for understanding and solving mathematical expressions: the order of operations.

When we have a mathematical expression with multiple operations, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division, it is important to follow a specific order to ensure that we get the correct answer. This order is known as the order of operations.

The order of operations is a set of rules that tells us which operations to perform first, second, third, and so on. By following these rules, we can avoid any confusion or ambiguity in our calculations.

The order of operations can be remembered using the acronym PEMDAS, which stands for:

- P: Parentheses (or brackets)
- E: Exponents (or powers)
- MD: Multiplication and Division (from left to right)
- AS: Addition and Subtraction (from left to right)

Let's break down each of these rules and understand how they work.

#### Parentheses (or brackets)

The first rule of the order of operations is to perform any calculations inside parentheses or brackets first. This allows us to isolate and simplify parts of the expression before moving on to other operations.

For example, consider the expression: $2 \times (3 + 4)$. According to the order of operations, we need to calculate the sum inside the parentheses first, which gives us $2 \times 7$. Then, we can multiply 2 by 7 to get the final answer of 14.

#### Exponents (or powers)

The next rule is to evaluate any exponents or powers in the expression. An exponent tells us how many times a number should be multiplied by itself.

For example, in the expression $2^3$, the base number is 2 and the exponent is 3. This means that we need to multiply 2 by itself three times: $2 \times 2 \times 2$. The result is 8.

#### Multiplication and Division (from left to right)

After evaluating any exponents, we move on to the next rule: multiplication and division. When we have multiple multiplication or division operations in an expression, we perform them from left to right.

For example, consider the expression $4 \div 2 \times 3$. According to the order of operations, we perform the division first, which gives us $2 \times 3$. Then, we multiply 2 by 3 to get the final answer of 6.

It's important to note that when we have both multiplication and division in an expression, we perform them from left to right, just like reading a book.

#### Addition and Subtraction (from left to right)

The final rule of the order of operations is addition and subtraction. Similar to multiplication and division, we perform these operations from left to right.

For example, consider the expression $5 + 3 - 2$. According to the order of operations, we perform the addition first, which gives us $8 - 2$. Then, we subtract 2 from 8 to get the final answer of 6.

By following the order of operations, we can ensure that our calculations are done correctly and consistently. It's important to remember these rules and apply them whenever we encounter mathematical expressions with multiple operations.

### Subsection: Real-life examples of order of operations

Now that we understand the order of operations, let's explore some real-life examples where these rules come into play.

#### Example 1: Baking a Cake

Imagine you are following a recipe to bake a cake. The recipe calls for mixing ingredients, baking the cake, and then adding frosting. If you were to mix the ingredients, bake the cake, and then add the frosting in a different order, the end result might not be what you expected. This is similar to the order of operations in math. Just as following the steps in the correct order is crucial for baking a delicious cake, following the order of operations is essential for obtaining accurate mathematical results.

#### Example 2: Planning a Trip

When planning a trip, you need to consider various factors such as transportation, accommodation, and activities. Let's say you want to calculate the total cost of your trip. You need to consider the cost of transportation, the cost of accommodation, and any additional expenses. If you were to add up the costs of accommodation and transportation before considering any additional expenses, you might end up with an incorrect total cost. This is similar to the order of operations in math. Just as considering the costs in the correct order is important for calculating the total cost of a trip, following the order of operations is crucial for obtaining accurate mathematical results.

Real-life examples like these help us understand the importance of the order of operations in both mathematical calculations and everyday situations. By following the order of operations, we can ensure that our calculations are done correctly and consistently, leading to accurate results.

### Section: More on order of operations

In the previous section, we learned about the order of operations and the acronym PEMDAS, which stands for Parentheses, Exponents, Multiplication and Division (from left to right), and Addition and Subtraction (from left to right). These rules help us determine the correct order in which to perform operations in a mathematical expression.

Now, let's dive deeper into the order of operations and explore some advanced concepts that will further enhance our understanding.

#### Advanced concepts in order of operations

While the basic rules of the order of operations cover most mathematical expressions, there are some advanced concepts that we need to be aware of. These concepts involve situations where multiple operations are present in an expression and require careful consideration.

##### Operations with fractions and decimals

When dealing with fractions and decimals, it's important to remember that the order of operations still applies. We need to perform calculations within parentheses or brackets first, evaluate any exponents, and then proceed with multiplication, division, addition, and subtraction.

For example, let's consider the expression: $\frac{1}{2} + \frac{3}{4} \times 2$. According to the order of operations, we need to multiply $\frac{3}{4}$ by 2 first, which gives us $\frac{3}{4} \times 2 = \frac{6}{4}$. Then, we can add $\frac{1}{2}$ to $\frac{6}{4}$ to get the final answer.

##### Nested parentheses or brackets

Sometimes, expressions may contain nested parentheses or brackets. In such cases, we need to work from the innermost set of parentheses or brackets outwards.

For example, consider the expression: $(2 + 3) \times (4 - 1)$. According to the order of operations, we need to calculate the sum inside the first set of parentheses, which gives us $2 + 3 = 5$. Then, we calculate the difference inside the second set of parentheses, which gives us $4 - 1 = 3$. Finally, we multiply 5 by 3 to get the final answer.

##### Operations with negative numbers

When negative numbers are involved in an expression, it's important to pay attention to the order of operations. The negative sign can be treated as a separate operation that needs to be performed before any other operations.

For example, let's consider the expression: $-3 \times (4 + 2)$. According to the order of operations, we need to calculate the sum inside the parentheses first, which gives us $4 + 2 = 6$. Then, we multiply $-3$ by 6 to get the final answer.

By understanding and applying these advanced concepts in the order of operations, we can confidently solve complex mathematical expressions and arrive at the correct answers.

In the next section, we will explore the topic of exponents in more detail and learn how to evaluate expressions with exponents.

### Section: More on order of operations

In the previous section, we learned about the order of operations and the acronym PEMDAS, which stands for Parentheses, Exponents, Multiplication and Division (from left to right), and Addition and Subtraction (from left to right). These rules help us determine the correct order in which to perform operations in a mathematical expression.

Now, let's dive deeper into the order of operations and explore some advanced concepts that will further enhance our understanding.

#### Advanced concepts in order of operations

While the basic rules of the order of operations cover most mathematical expressions, there are some advanced concepts that we need to be aware of. These concepts involve situations where multiple operations are present in an expression and require careful consideration.

##### Operations with fractions and decimals

When dealing with fractions and decimals, it's important to remember that the order of operations still applies. We need to perform calculations within parentheses or brackets first, evaluate any exponents, and then proceed with multiplication, division, addition, and subtraction.

For example, let's consider the expression: $\frac{1}{2} + \frac{3}{4} \times 2$. According to the order of operations, we need to multiply $\frac{3}{4}$ by 2 first, which gives us $\frac{3}{4} \times 2 = \frac{6}{4}$. Then, we can add $\frac{1}{2}$ to $\frac{6}{4}$ to get the final answer.

##### Nested parentheses or brackets

Sometimes, expressions may contain nested parentheses or brackets. In such cases, we need to work from the innermost set of parentheses or brackets outwards.

For example, consider the expression: $(2 + 3) \times (4 - 1)$. According to the order of operations, we need to calculate the sum inside the first set of parentheses, which gives us $2 + 3 = 5$. Then, we calculate the difference inside the second set of parentheses, which gives us $4 - 1 = 3$. Finally, we multiply 5 by 3 to get the final answer.

#### Challenging problems on order of operations

Now that we have a solid understanding of the order of operations, let's put our skills to the test with some challenging problems. These problems will require us to carefully apply the rules of the order of operations to arrive at the correct answers.

Problem 1: Simplify the expression $3 + 4 \times 2 - (5 - 1)$.

To solve this problem, we start by evaluating the expression inside the parentheses: $5 - 1 = 4$. Next, we perform the multiplication: $4 \times 2 = 8$. Then, we perform the addition and subtraction from left to right: $3 + 8 - 4 = 7$. Therefore, the simplified expression is 7.

Problem 2: Simplify the expression $2 \times (3 + 4) - 5 \times 2$.

To solve this problem, we start by evaluating the expression inside the parentheses: $3 + 4 = 7$. Next, we perform the multiplication: $2 \times 7 = 14$. Then, we perform the multiplication and subtraction from left to right: $14 - 5 \times 2 = 14 - 10 = 4$. Therefore, the simplified expression is 4.

By practicing these challenging problems, we can further strengthen our understanding of the order of operations and become more confident in solving complex mathematical expressions.

Now that we have explored more advanced concepts and solved challenging problems related to the order of operations, we are ready to move on to the next chapter, where we will delve into the fascinating world of exponents.

We have learned how to simplify expressions with exponents, including multiplying and dividing numbers with the same base and raising a power to a power. Additionally, we have discussed the order of operations, which is a set of rules that dictate the sequence in which mathematical operations should be performed. By following the order of operations, we can ensure that our calculations are accurate and consistent.

Understanding exponents and the order of operations is crucial for success in mathematics. These concepts are foundational and are used in various areas of math, including algebra, geometry, and calculus. By mastering these concepts, students will be equipped with the necessary skills to solve complex mathematical problems and tackle more advanced topics in the future.

## Chapter: Negative numbers

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the concept of negative numbers. Negative numbers are an essential part of mathematics and play a crucial role in various mathematical operations. Understanding negative numbers is fundamental for students to excel in their mathematical journey.

Negative numbers represent quantities that are less than zero. They are denoted by a minus sign (-) placed before the number. For example, -5 represents a number that is five units less than zero. Negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties and operations involving negative numbers. We will learn how to add, subtract, multiply, and divide negative numbers. Additionally, we will discuss the concept of absolute value, which helps us determine the distance of a number from zero, regardless of its sign.

Understanding negative numbers is not only important in mathematics but also in real-life situations. Negative numbers are used in various fields such as finance, temperature, and coordinates in a coordinate plane. By mastering negative numbers, students will be equipped with a powerful tool to solve a wide range of mathematical problems.

So, let's dive into the world of negative numbers and discover their significance in mathematics and beyond. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of negative numbers and be able to confidently apply them in various mathematical contexts.

### Section: Intro to negative numbers

Negative numbers are an important concept in mathematics that we will explore in this chapter. In the previous section, we learned that negative numbers represent quantities that are less than zero. They are denoted by a minus sign (-) placed before the number.

To understand negative numbers, let's imagine a number line. A number line is a straight line that extends infinitely in both directions. On the number line, positive numbers are represented to the right of zero, while negative numbers are represented to the left of zero.

Negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line. For example, if we start at zero and move five units to the left, we reach the number -5. This means that -5 is five units less than zero.

Negative numbers are used in various real-life situations. For instance, in finance, negative numbers can represent debts or losses. In temperature, negative numbers can represent temperatures below zero. In a coordinate plane, negative numbers can represent points to the left or below the origin.

Now that we have a basic understanding of negative numbers, let's move on to exploring their properties and operations. In the next section, we will learn how to add, subtract, multiply, and divide negative numbers. We will also discuss the concept of absolute value, which helps us determine the distance of a number from zero, regardless of its sign.

By mastering negative numbers, you will gain a powerful tool to solve a wide range of mathematical problems. So, let's continue our journey into the world of negative numbers and discover their significance in mathematics and beyond.

### Section: Intro to negative numbers

Negative numbers are an important concept in mathematics that we will explore in this chapter. In the previous section, we learned that negative numbers represent quantities that are less than zero. They are denoted by a minus sign (-) placed before the number.

To understand negative numbers, let's imagine a number line. A number line is a straight line that extends infinitely in both directions. On the number line, positive numbers are represented to the right of zero, while negative numbers are represented to the left of zero.

Negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line. For example, if we start at zero and move five units to the left, we reach the number -5. This means that -5 is five units less than zero.

Negative numbers are used in various real-life situations. Let's explore some examples to understand how negative numbers are applied in everyday life.

#### Real-life examples of negative numbers

1. **Finances**: Negative numbers can represent debts or losses in finance. For example, if you owe $50 to a friend, it can be represented as -50. Similarly, if a company incurs a loss of $100, it can be represented as -100.

2. **Temperature**: Negative numbers can represent temperatures below zero. In colder regions, temperatures often drop below zero degrees Celsius or Fahrenheit. For instance, if the temperature is -10 degrees Celsius, it means it is 10 degrees below freezing point.

3. **Elevations**: Negative numbers can also represent elevations below sea level. In some places, there are areas that lie below sea level, such as the Dead Sea. The elevation of the Dead Sea is approximately -430 meters, indicating that it is 430 meters below sea level.

4. **Coordinates**: In a coordinate plane, negative numbers can represent points to the left or below the origin. The origin is the point (0, 0) where the x-axis and y-axis intersect. If a point is to the left of the origin, its x-coordinate will be negative. Similarly, if a point is below the origin, its y-coordinate will be negative.

These are just a few examples of how negative numbers are used in real-life situations. By understanding negative numbers, we can make sense of these scenarios and solve problems related to them.

Now that we have explored some real-life examples, let's move on to the next section where we will learn how to perform operations with negative numbers. We will discover how to add, subtract, multiply, and divide negative numbers. We will also discuss the concept of absolute value, which helps us determine the distance of a number from zero, regardless of its sign.

By mastering negative numbers, you will gain a powerful tool to solve a wide range of mathematical problems. So, let's continue our journey into the world of negative numbers and discover their significance in mathematics and beyond.

### Section: Negative symbol as opposite

In the previous section, we learned that negative numbers represent quantities that are less than zero. We also discussed how negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line. Now, let's delve deeper into understanding the negative symbol as the opposite.

When we see a negative symbol (-) placed before a number, it indicates that the number is the opposite of what it would be without the negative symbol. In other words, the negative symbol changes the direction or sign of the number.

To illustrate this concept, let's consider a positive number, such as 5. If we add a negative symbol before 5, it becomes -5. This means that -5 is the opposite of 5. On a number line, 5 would be represented to the right of zero, while -5 would be represented to the left of zero.

Similarly, if we have a negative number, such as -3, and we remove the negative symbol, it becomes 3. This means that 3 is the opposite of -3. On a number line, -3 would be represented to the left of zero, while 3 would be represented to the right of zero.

Understanding the negative symbol as the opposite is crucial in various mathematical operations involving negative numbers. For example, when adding a positive number and a negative number, we can think of it as finding the difference between the two numbers. The negative symbol indicates that the two numbers are in opposite directions, and the result will be the distance between them.

Let's consider an example: 5 + (-3). We can interpret this as starting at 5 and moving 3 units in the opposite direction, which brings us to 2. Therefore, 5 + (-3) equals 2.

Similarly, when subtracting a negative number, we can think of it as adding the opposite of that negative number. For example, 7 - (-2) can be interpreted as starting at 7 and adding 2 units in the opposite direction, which brings us to 9. Therefore, 7 - (-2) equals 9.

Understanding the negative symbol as the opposite allows us to make sense of these operations and solve problems involving negative numbers more effectively.

In the next subsection, we will explore some practice problems to reinforce our understanding of the negative symbol as the opposite.

### Section: Negative symbol as opposite

In the previous section, we learned that negative numbers represent quantities that are less than zero. ### Section: Rational numbers on the number line

In the previous section, we learned about negative numbers and how they represent quantities that are less than zero. We also discussed how negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line. Now, let's explore another important concept related to numbers on the number line - rational numbers.

Rational numbers are numbers that can be expressed as a fraction, where the numerator and denominator are both integers. They can be positive, negative, or zero. Examples of rational numbers include 1/2, -3/4, and 0.

To better understand rational numbers, let's plot them on the number line. The number line is a horizontal line that extends infinitely in both directions. The middle point of the number line is zero, and positive numbers are located to the right of zero, while negative numbers are located to the left of zero.

To plot a rational number on the number line, we need to consider its value in relation to zero. If the rational number is positive, we move to the right of zero, and if it is negative, we move to the left of zero. The distance from zero represents the magnitude or absolute value of the rational number.

For example, let's plot the rational number 1/2 on the number line. Since 1/2 is positive, we move to the right of zero. The distance from zero is 1/2, so we mark a point on the number line at 1/2.

Similarly, let's plot the rational number -3/4 on the number line. Since -3/4 is negative, we move to the left of zero. The distance from zero is 3/4, so we mark a point on the number line at -3/4.

It's important to note that rational numbers can also be represented as decimals. For example, 1/2 is equivalent to 0.5 and -3/4 is equivalent to -0.75. When plotting rational numbers as decimals on the number line, we follow the same principles - positive decimals are located to the right of zero, and negative decimals are located to the left of zero.

Plotting rational numbers on the number line helps us visualize their position and understand their relationship to other numbers. It also allows us to compare and order rational numbers based on their magnitude.

In the next section, we will explore operations with rational numbers and how to perform calculations involving them.

### Section: Rational numbers on the number line

In the previous section, we learned about negative numbers and how they represent quantities that are less than zero. The distance from zero represents the magnitude or absolute value of the rational number.

### Section: Comparing negative numbers

In the previous section, we learned about negative numbers and how they represent quantities that are less than zero. We also discussed how negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line. Now, let's delve deeper into comparing negative numbers.

#### Understanding how to compare negative numbers

When comparing negative numbers, it's important to consider their positions on the number line. As we move to the left on the number line, the numbers become smaller and more negative. Conversely, as we move to the right, the numbers become larger and less negative.

To compare negative numbers, we can use the concept of absolute value. The absolute value of a number is its distance from zero on the number line, regardless of its sign. For example, the absolute value of -5 is 5, and the absolute value of -10 is 10.

To compare two negative numbers, we compare their absolute values. The negative number with the greater absolute value is actually the smaller number. Let's look at an example:

Consider the numbers -3 and -7. Both numbers are negative, but -3 is closer to zero on the number line than -7. Therefore, -3 is actually greater than -7.

We can also compare negative numbers to positive numbers. In this case, the positive number is always greater than the negative number. For example, -5 is less than 3 because 3 is a positive number and -5 is a negative number.

It's important to note that when comparing negative numbers, we only consider their absolute values. The sign of the number does not affect the comparison. For example, -3 is greater than -5, even though -3 is negative and -5 is positive.

In summary, when comparing negative numbers:
- Compare their absolute values.
- The negative number with the greater absolute value is actually the smaller number.
- Positive numbers are always greater than negative numbers.

Now that we understand how to compare negative numbers, let's move on to the next section where we will explore operations with negative numbers.

### Section: Comparing negative numbers

In the previous section, we learned about negative numbers and how they represent quantities that are less than zero. We also discussed how negative numbers can be thought of as numbers that move in the opposite direction on the number line. Now, let's delve deeper into comparing negative numbers.

#### Practice problems on comparing negative numbers

1. Compare the following pairs of negative numbers and write the greater number:
   a) -8 and -3
   b) -12 and -15
   c) -20 and -10

2. Compare the following pairs of negative numbers and write the smaller number:
   a) -6 and -2
   b) -9 and -5
   c) -14 and -7

   b) -10 and -10
   c) -2 and -2

### Section: Ordering rational numbers

In the previous section, we learned about comparing negative numbers and how their positions on the number line can help us determine their relative values. Now, let's take our understanding a step further and explore how to order rational numbers.

#### Understanding how to order rational numbers

Ordering rational numbers involves arranging them in a sequence from least to greatest or from greatest to least. To do this, we can use the concept of the number line. 

Just like with negative numbers, we can represent rational numbers on a number line. The number line helps us visualize the relative positions of different rational numbers and makes it easier to compare and order them.

To order rational numbers, we follow these steps:

1. Plot the rational numbers on a number line.
2. Identify the position of each rational number on the number line.
3. Compare the positions of the rational numbers to determine their order.

Let's look at an example to understand this better:

Consider the rational numbers -2/3, 1/2, and -5/6. To order these rational numbers, we first plot them on a number line:

```
   -2/3        1/2        -5/6
    |          |          |
----|----------|----------|----
```

Next, we identify the positions of each rational number on the number line. In this case, -5/6 is the smallest because it is the furthest to the left, followed by -2/3, and then 1/2, which is the furthest to the right.

Finally, we compare the positions of the rational numbers to determine their order. In this example, the order from least to greatest is:

-5/6 < -2/3 < 1/2

By following these steps, we can order rational numbers and understand their relative values.

It's important to note that when ordering rational numbers, we consider both positive and negative numbers. Positive numbers are always greater than negative numbers, and within each category, we compare the absolute values to determine their order.

In summary, to order rational numbers:
- Plot the rational numbers on a number line.
- Identify the positions of the rational numbers on the number line.
- Compare the positions to determine the order, considering both positive and negative numbers.

Now that we understand how to order rational numbers, let's practice this concept with some exercises in the next section.

### Section: Ordering rational numbers

In the previous section, we learned about comparing negative numbers and how their positions on the number line can help us determine their relative values. Now, let's take our understanding a step further and explore how to order rational numbers.

#### Understanding how to order rational numbers

Ordering rational numbers involves arranging them in a sequence from least to greatest or from greatest to least. To do this, we can use the concept of the number line. 

Just like with negative numbers, we can represent rational numbers on a number line. The number line helps us visualize the relative positions of different rational numbers and makes it easier to compare and order them.

To order rational numbers, we follow these steps:

1. Plot the rational numbers on a number line.
2. Identify the position of each rational number on the number line.
3. Compare the positions of the rational numbers to determine their order.

Let's look at an example to understand this better:

Consider the rational numbers $-\frac{2}{3}$, $\frac{1}{2}$, and $-\frac{5}{6}$. To order these rational numbers, we first plot them on a number line:

```
   -2/3        1/2        -5/6
    |          |          |
----|----------|----------|----
```

Next, we identify the positions of each rational number on the number line. In this case, $-\frac{5}{6}$ is the smallest because it is the furthest to the left, followed by $-\frac{2}{3}$, and then $\frac{1}{2}$, which is the furthest to the right.

Finally, we compare the positions of the rational numbers to determine their order. In this example, the order from least to greatest is:

$-\frac{5}{6} < -\frac{2}{3} < \frac{1}{2}$

By following these steps, we can order rational numbers and understand their relative values.

It's important to note that when ordering rational numbers, we consider both positive and negative numbers. Positive numbers are always greater than negative numbers, and within each category, we compare the absolute values to determine their order.

In summary, to order rational numbers:

1. Plot the rational numbers on a number line.
2. Identify the position of each rational number on the number line.
3. Compare the positions of the rational numbers to determine their order.

Now that we understand how to order rational numbers, let's practice solving some problems to reinforce our knowledge.

#### Practice problems on ordering rational numbers

1. Order the following rational numbers from least to greatest: $-\frac{3}{4}$, $\frac{2}{5}$, $-\frac{1}{2}$.

2. Order the following rational numbers from greatest to least: $\frac{7}{8}$, $-\frac{3}{5}$, $\frac{4}{7}$.

Take your time to solve these problems and check your answers.

### Section: Intro to absolute value

In the previous section, we learned about ordering rational numbers and how to determine their relative values using the number line. Now, let's explore another important concept in mathematics - absolute value.

#### Understanding the concept of absolute value

Absolute value is a mathematical concept that helps us understand the distance between a number and zero on the number line. It is denoted by two vertical bars surrounding the number. For example, the absolute value of 5 is written as $|5|$.

The absolute value of a number is always positive or zero. It tells us how far a number is from zero, regardless of whether the number is positive or negative. For example, the absolute value of -5 is also 5, because -5 is 5 units away from zero on the number line.

To calculate the absolute value of a number, we can follow these steps:

1. If the number is positive, the absolute value is the same as the original number. For example, $|7| = 7$.
2. If the number is negative, we remove the negative sign to make it positive. For example, $|-7| = 7$.

Let's look at some examples to understand this better:

Example 1:
Calculate the absolute value of -3.

Solution:
Since -3 is a negative number, we remove the negative sign to make it positive. Therefore, $|-3| = 3$.

Example 2:
Calculate the absolute value of 8.

Solution:
Since 8 is a positive number, the absolute value is the same as the original number. Therefore, $|8| = 8$.

Example 3:
Calculate the absolute value of 0.

Solution:
Since 0 is neither positive nor negative, the absolute value is 0 itself. Therefore, $|0| = 0$.

Understanding absolute value is crucial in various mathematical concepts, such as solving equations, working with inequalities, and understanding the distance between numbers. It allows us to focus on the magnitude of a number without considering its sign.

In the next section, we will explore how to apply the concept of absolute value in solving mathematical problems.

### Section: Intro to absolute value

In the previous section, we learned about ordering rational numbers and how to determine their relative values using the number line. Now, let's explore another important concept in mathematics - absolute value.

#### Understanding the concept of absolute value

Absolute value is a mathematical concept that helps us understand the distance between a number and zero on the number line. It is denoted by two vertical bars surrounding the number. For example, the absolute value of 5 is written as $|5|$.

The absolute value of a number is always positive or zero. It tells us how far a number is from zero, regardless of whether the number is positive or negative. For example, the absolute value of -5 is also 5, because -5 is 5 units away from zero on the number line.

To calculate the absolute value of a number, we can follow these steps:

1. If the number is positive, the absolute value is the same as the original number. For example, $|7| = 7$.
2. If the number is negative, we remove the negative sign to make it positive. For example, $|-7| = 7$.

Let's look at some examples to understand this better:

Example 1:
Calculate the absolute value of -3.

Solution:
Since -3 is a negative number, we remove the negative sign to make it positive. Therefore, $|-3| = 3$.

Example 2:
Calculate the absolute value of 8.

Solution:
Since 8 is a positive number, the absolute value is the same as the original number. Therefore, $|8| = 8$.

Example 3:
Calculate the absolute value of 0.

Solution:
Since 0 is neither positive nor negative, the absolute value is 0 itself. Therefore, $|0| = 0$.

Understanding absolute value is crucial in various mathematical concepts, such as solving equations, working with inequalities, and understanding the distance between numbers. It allows us to focus on the magnitude of a number without considering its sign.

#### Real-life examples of absolute value

Now that we understand the concept of absolute value, let's explore some real-life examples where it can be applied.

1. Temperature: Absolute value can be used to calculate the difference between two temperatures. For example, if the temperature is -5 degrees Celsius and it increases to 10 degrees Celsius, we can calculate the absolute value of the temperature change as $|10 - (-5)| = 15$ degrees Celsius.

2. Distance: Absolute value can also be used to calculate the distance between two points on a map. For example, if we have two points A and B, and the coordinates of A are (-3, 2) and the coordinates of B are (4, 5), we can calculate the distance between A and B using the absolute value as $|4 - (-3)| + |5 - 2| = 7 + 3 = 10$ units.

3. Finances: Absolute value can be used in financial calculations, such as calculating the difference between income and expenses. For example, if someone earns $1000 and spends $800, we can calculate the absolute value of the difference as $|1000 - 800| = 200$ dollars.

These are just a few examples of how absolute value can be applied in real-life situations. By understanding absolute value, we can solve various problems and make sense of numerical data in different contexts.

In the next section, we will explore how to apply the concept of absolute value in solving mathematical problems.

### Section: Comparing absolute values

In the previous section, we learned about absolute value and how it helps us understand the distance between a number and zero on the number line. Now, let's explore how we can compare the absolute values of different numbers.

#### Understanding how to compare absolute values

When comparing absolute values, we are interested in determining which number is farther from zero on the number line. To do this, we can follow these steps:

1. Identify the numbers whose absolute values we want to compare.
2. Calculate the absolute value of each number.
3. Compare the absolute values.

Let's look at some examples to understand this better:

Example 1:
Compare the absolute values of -5 and 3.

Solution:
To compare the absolute values of -5 and 3, we first calculate their absolute values. The absolute value of -5 is 5, and the absolute value of 3 is also 3. Since 5 is greater than 3, we can say that |-5| > |3|.

Example 2:
Compare the absolute values of -2 and -7.

Solution:
To compare the absolute values of -2 and -7, we first calculate their absolute values. The absolute value of -2 is 2, and the absolute value of -7 is 7. Since 7 is greater than 2, we can say that |-2| < |-7|.

Example 3:
Compare the absolute values of 0 and 9.

Solution:
To compare the absolute values of 0 and 9, we first calculate their absolute values. The absolute value of 0 is 0, and the absolute value of 9 is 9. Since 9 is greater than 0, we can say that |0| < |9|.

Comparing absolute values is useful in various mathematical concepts, such as solving equations and working with inequalities. It allows us to determine the magnitude of a number without considering its sign.

Remember, when comparing absolute values, we are only interested in the distance from zero on the number line. The sign of the number does not affect the comparison.

Now that we understand how to compare absolute values, let's move on to the next section where we will explore another important concept related to negative numbers.

## Chapter: Variables & expressions

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Variables & expressions! In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of variables and expressions in 6th grade math. 

Variables are symbols that represent unknown quantities or values. They are commonly used in mathematics to solve equations and express relationships between different quantities. Understanding variables is crucial in developing problem-solving skills and mathematical reasoning.

Expressions, on the other hand, are mathematical phrases that combine numbers, variables, and operations. They can be as simple as a single number or as complex as a combination of variables and operations. Expressions are used to represent mathematical relationships and can be evaluated to obtain a numerical value.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into various topics related to variables and expressions. We will learn how to identify variables in equations, solve equations with variables, and simplify expressions using the order of operations. We will also explore the concept of substitution, where we replace variables with specific values to evaluate expressions.

By mastering the concepts covered in this chapter, you will gain a solid foundation in working with variables and expressions. These skills will not only be useful in 6th grade math but also in higher-level mathematics and real-life problem-solving situations.

So, let's dive in and start mastering variables and expressions!

### Section: Parts of algebraic expressions

In the previous section, we learned about variables and expressions in mathematics. Now, let's dive deeper into the parts of algebraic expressions.

An algebraic expression is a mathematical phrase that contains numbers, variables, and operations. It represents a relationship between quantities and can be evaluated to obtain a numerical value. Understanding the different parts of an algebraic expression is essential for simplifying and solving equations.

#### Terms

In an algebraic expression, terms are the building blocks. A term can be a single number, a variable, or a combination of both. Terms are separated by addition or subtraction symbols.

For example, in the expression $3x + 2y - 5$, the terms are $3x$, $2y$, and $-5$. Each term represents a quantity or a value.

#### Coefficients

The coefficient of a term is the number that multiplies the variable. It tells us how many times the variable appears in the expression. If a term does not have a variable, the coefficient is simply the number itself.

In the expression $3x + 2y - 5$, the coefficient of $3x$ is 3, the coefficient of $2y$ is 2, and the coefficient of $-5$ is -5.

#### Variables

Variables are symbols that represent unknown quantities or values. They are usually represented by letters such as $x$, $y$, or $z$. Variables allow us to solve equations and express relationships between different quantities.

In the expression $3x + 2y - 5$, $x$ and $y$ are variables. We can assign specific values to these variables to evaluate the expression.

#### Constants

Constants are fixed values that do not change. They are represented by numbers in an algebraic expression. In the expression $3x + 2y - 5$, -5 is a constant.

#### Operations

Operations are mathematical actions performed on the terms of an expression. The most common operations are addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division.

In the expression $3x + 2y - 5$, addition and subtraction are the operations used to combine the terms.

#### Combining Like Terms

When simplifying an algebraic expression, we often need to combine like terms. Like terms have the same variable(s) raised to the same power(s).

For example, in the expression $3x + 2y - 5 + 2x - 3y$, the like terms are $3x$ and $2x$, and $2y$ and $-3y$. We can combine these like terms to simplify the expression.

Understanding the different parts of an algebraic expression is crucial for solving equations and simplifying expressions. In the next section, we will explore how to solve equations with variables.

### Section: Parts of algebraic expressions

In the previous section, we learned about variables and expressions in mathematics. Now, let's dive deeper into the parts of algebraic expressions.

An algebraic expression is a mathematical phrase that contains numbers, variables, and operations. It represents a relationship between quantities and can be evaluated to obtain a numerical value. Now that we have a good understanding of the different parts of an algebraic expression, let's practice identifying them in various examples.

### Subsection: Practice problems on parts of algebraic expressions

Let's solve some practice problems to reinforce our understanding of the different parts of algebraic expressions.

**Problem 1:** Identify the terms, coefficients, variables, constants, and operations in the expression $4a - 2b + 7$.

**Solution:**
- Terms: $4a$, $-2b$, and $7$
- Coefficients: 4 and -2
- Variables: $a$ and $b$
- Constants: 7
- Operations: Subtraction and addition

**Problem 2:** Identify the terms, coefficients, variables, constants, and operations in the expression $2x^2 - 3y + 5z$.

**Solution:**
- Terms: $2x^2$, $-3y$, and $5z$
- Coefficients: 2, -3, and 5
- Variables: $x$, $y$, and $z$
- Constants: None
- Operations: Subtraction and addition

**Problem 3:** Identify the terms, coefficients, variables, constants, and operations in the expression $6 - 2x + 4y - z$.

**Solution:**
- Terms: 6, $-2x$, $4y$, and $-z$
- Coefficients: -2 and 4
- Variables: $x$, $y$, and $z$
- Constants: 6
- Operations: Subtraction and addition

Great job! By practicing these problems, you have become more familiar with identifying the different parts of algebraic expressions. This knowledge will be crucial as we move forward in mastering 6th-grade math.

### Section: Substitution & evaluating expressions

In the previous section, we learned about the different parts of algebraic expressions, such as terms, coefficients, variables, constants, and operations. Now, let's explore how we can substitute values into expressions and evaluate them.

#### Understanding substitution

Substitution is the process of replacing variables in an expression with specific values. This allows us to find the numerical value of the expression when the variables are assigned certain values. Substitution is a powerful tool that helps us solve equations and understand the relationship between different quantities.

For example, consider the expression $3x + 2y - 5$. If we substitute $x = 2$ and $y = 4$ into this expression, we can find its value by replacing $x$ with 2 and $y$ with 4:

$$
3(2) + 2(4) - 5
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
6 + 8 - 5
$$

$$
9
$$

So, when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$, the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ evaluates to 9.

#### Evaluating expressions

Evaluation is the process of finding the numerical value of an expression. It involves performing the necessary operations and simplifications to obtain a single value.

To evaluate an expression, we follow the order of operations, also known as PEMDAS (Parentheses, Exponents, Multiplication and Division from left to right, and Addition and Subtraction from left to right). This ensures that we perform the operations in the correct order.

Let's evaluate the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$:

$$
3(2) + 2(4) - 5
$$

Following the order of operations, we first perform the multiplications:

$$
6 + 8 - 5
$$

Next, we perform the additions:

$$
14 - 5
$$

Finally, we perform the subtraction:

$$
9
$$

Therefore, when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$, the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ evaluates to 9.

Substitution and evaluation are fundamental concepts in mathematics that allow us to solve equations, simplify expressions, and understand the relationship between different quantities. By mastering these skills, you will be able to tackle more complex mathematical problems with confidence.

### Section: Substitution & evaluating expressions

In the previous section, we learned about the different parts of algebraic expressions, such as terms, coefficients, variables, constants, and operations. Now, let's explore how we can substitute values into expressions and evaluate them.

#### Understanding substitution

Substitution is the process of replacing variables in an expression with specific values. This allows us to find the numerical value of the expression when the variables are assigned certain values. Substitution is a powerful tool that helps us solve equations and understand the relationship between different quantities.

$$

$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$

$$

$$
9
$$

So, when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$, the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ evaluates to 9.

#### Evaluating expressions

Evaluation is the process of finding the numerical value of an expression. It involves performing the necessary operations and simplifications to obtain a single value.

To evaluate an expression, we follow the order of operations, also known as PEMDAS (Parentheses, Exponents, Multiplication and Division from left to right, and Addition and Subtraction from left to right). This ensures that we perform the operations in the correct order.

Let's evaluate the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$:

$$
3(2) + 2(4) - 5
$$

Following the order of operations, we first perform the multiplications:

$$
6 + 8 - 5
$$

Next, we perform the additions:

$$
14 - 5
$$

Finally, we perform the subtraction:

$$
9
$$

Therefore, when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$, the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ evaluates to 9.

2. Evaluate the expression $4a - 2b + 7c$ when $a = 3$, $b = 2$, and $c = 1$.

### Section: Evaluating expressions with multiple variables

In the previous section, we learned about substitution and how it allows us to find the numerical value of an expression when the variables are assigned specific values. Now, let's explore how we can evaluate expressions that involve multiple variables.

#### Understanding expressions with multiple variables

Expressions with multiple variables are mathematical statements that involve more than one variable. These variables can represent different quantities or unknown values. By evaluating these expressions, we can find their numerical value based on the given values of the variables.

For example, consider the expression $3x + 2y - 5$. This expression involves two variables, $x$ and $y$. To evaluate this expression, we need to substitute specific values for $x$ and $y$ and perform the necessary operations.

Let's say we want to evaluate the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$. We can substitute these values into the expression:

$$
3(2) + 2(4) - 5
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
6 + 8 - 5
$$

$$
9
$$

Therefore, when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$, the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ evaluates to 9.

#### Evaluating expressions with multiple variables

To evaluate expressions with multiple variables, we follow the same principles as evaluating expressions with a single variable. We substitute the given values for the variables and perform the necessary operations according to the order of operations (PEMDAS).

Let's practice evaluating an expression with multiple variables. Consider the expression $2x - 3y + 7$ when $x = 5$ and $y = 2$. We can substitute these values into the expression:

$$
2(5) - 3(2) + 7
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
10 - 6 + 7
$$

$$
11
$$

Therefore, when $x = 5$ and $y = 2$, the expression $2x - 3y + 7$ evaluates to 11.

Now, let's try solving some practice problems to reinforce our understanding of evaluating expressions with multiple variables.

#### Practice problem
```
Evaluate the expression $4x + 3y - 2z$ when $x = 3$, $y = 2$, and $z = 1$.
```

To evaluate this expression, we substitute the given values for $x$, $y$, and $z$:

$$
4(3) + 3(2) - 2(1)
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
12 + 6 - 2
$$

$$
16
$$

Therefore, when $x = 3$, $y = 2$, and $z = 1$, the expression $4x + 3y - 2z$ evaluates to 16.

By practicing evaluating expressions with multiple variables, we can become more comfortable with solving problems that involve these types of expressions. This will help us in various mathematical applications and problem-solving situations.

### Section: Evaluating expressions with multiple variables

Expressions with multiple variables are mathematical statements that involve more than one variable. These variables can represent different quantities or unknown values. By evaluating these expressions, we can find their numerical value based on the given values of the variables.
