$$
3(2) + 2(4) - 5
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
6 + 8 - 5
$$

$$
9
$$

Therefore, when $x = 2$ and $y = 4$, the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ evaluates to 9.

#### Evaluating expressions with multiple variables

To evaluate expressions with multiple variables, we follow the same principles as evaluating expressions with a single variable. We substitute the given values for the variables and perform the necessary operations according to the order of operations (PEMDAS).

Let's practice evaluating an expression with multiple variables. Consider the expression $2x - 3y + 7$ when $x = 5$ and $y = 2$. We can substitute these values into the expression:

$$
2(5) - 3(2) + 7
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
10 - 6 + 7
$$

$$
11
$$

Therefore, when $x = 5$ and $y = 2$, the expression $2x - 3y + 7$ evaluates to 11.

#### Practice problems on expressions with multiple variables

Now, let's solve some practice problems to reinforce our understanding of evaluating expressions with multiple variables.

##### Practice Problem 1:

Evaluate the expression $4x + 3y - 2$ when $x = 3$ and $y = 5$.

##### Solution:

Substituting the given values into the expression, we get:

$$
4(3) + 3(5) - 2
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
12 + 15 - 2
$$

$$
25
$$

Therefore, when $x = 3$ and $y = 5$, the expression $4x + 3y - 2$ evaluates to 25.

##### Practice Problem 2:

Evaluate the expression $2x^2 - 3xy + 4y^2$ when $x = 2$ and $y = 3$.

##### Solution:

Substituting the given values into the expression, we get:

$$
2(2^2) - 3(2)(3) + 4(3^2)
$$

Simplifying this expression, we get:

$$
2(4) - 3(6) + 4(9)
$$

$$
8 - 18 + 36
$$

$$
26
$$

Therefore, when $x = 2$ and $y = 3$, the expression $2x^2 - 3xy + 4y^2$ evaluates to 26.

Keep practicing evaluating expressions with multiple variables to strengthen your understanding of this concept.

### Section: Expression value intuition

In the previous section, we learned about evaluating expressions with multiple variables by substituting specific values for the variables and performing the necessary operations. This allowed us to find the numerical value of the expression based on the given values of the variables. Now, let's develop our intuition for understanding expression values even further.

#### Developing intuition for expression values

Developing intuition for expression values involves understanding how different operations and variables interact within an expression. By recognizing patterns and relationships, we can make predictions about the value of an expression without having to perform all the calculations.

One way to develop intuition for expression values is to analyze the individual components of the expression. Let's consider the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ as an example. We can break down this expression into three parts: $3x$, $2y$, and $-5$. 

The term $3x$ represents three times the value of $x$. If we increase the value of $x$, the value of $3x$ will also increase. Similarly, if we decrease the value of $x$, the value of $3x$ will decrease. 

The term $2y$ represents two times the value of $y$. Just like with $3x$, if we increase the value of $y$, the value of $2y$ will increase. If we decrease the value of $y$, the value of $2y$ will decrease.

The constant term $-5$ does not depend on any variables. It remains the same regardless of the values of $x$ and $y$.

By understanding how each component of the expression behaves, we can make predictions about the overall value of the expression. For example, if $x$ and $y$ are both positive, we can expect the value of the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ to be positive. On the other hand, if $x$ and $y$ are both negative, we can expect the value of the expression to be negative.

Developing intuition for expression values takes practice and familiarity with different mathematical operations. As you continue to work with expressions and solve problems, you will become more comfortable in predicting the value of an expression based on its components.

#### Summary

In this section, we explored how to develop intuition for expression values. By analyzing the individual components of an expression and understanding how they interact, we can make predictions about the overall value of the expression. This intuition allows us to gain a deeper understanding of mathematical concepts and solve problems more efficiently. In the next section, we will delve into the topic of simplifying expressions.

### Section: Expression value intuition

In the previous section, we learned about evaluating expressions with multiple variables by substituting specific values for the variables and performing the necessary operations. This allowed us to find the numerical value of the expression based on the given values of the variables. Now, let's develop our intuition for understanding expression values even further.

#### Developing intuition for expression values

Developing intuition for expression values involves understanding how different operations and variables interact within an expression. By recognizing patterns and relationships, we can make predictions about the value of an expression without having to perform all the calculations.

One way to develop intuition for expression values is to analyze the individual components of the expression. Let's consider the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ as an example. We can break down this expression into three parts: $3x$, $2y$, and $-5$. 

The term $3x$ represents three times the value of $x$. If we increase the value of $x$, the value of $3x$ will also increase. Similarly, if we decrease the value of $x$, the value of $3x$ will decrease. 

The term $2y$ represents two times the value of $y$. Just like with $3x$, if we increase the value of $y$, the value of $2y$ will increase. If we decrease the value of $y$, the value of $2y$ will decrease.

The constant term $-5$ does not depend on any variables. It remains the same regardless of the values of $x$ and $y$.

By understanding how each component of the expression behaves, we can make predictions about the overall value of the expression. For example, if $x$ and $y$ are both positive, we can expect the value of the expression $3x + 2y - 5$ to be positive. On the other hand, if $x$ and $y$ are both negative, we can expect the value of the expression to be negative.

Developing intuition for expression values takes practice and familiarity with different mathematical operations. As you continue to work with expressions and solve problems, you will become more comfortable in predicting the value of an expression without having to perform all the calculations.

Now, let's practice our skills in expression value intuition with some practice problems.

#### Practice problems on expression value intuition

1. Evaluate the expression $4x - 3y + 2$ when $x = 5$ and $y = 2$.
2. Find the value of the expression $2x^2 - 3xy + 4y^2$ when $x = 3$ and $y = 2$.
3. Simplify the expression $2(x + 3) - 3(2x - 1)$.

Take your time to solve these problems and remember to analyze the individual components of the expressions to make predictions about their values.

### Section: Evaluating expressions word problems

In the previous section, we learned how to evaluate expressions by substituting specific values for the variables and performing the necessary operations. This allowed us to find the numerical value of the expression based on the given values of the variables. Now, let's apply our knowledge of evaluating expressions to solve word problems.

Word problems involving expression evaluation often require us to translate a real-life situation into an algebraic expression and then evaluate that expression to find the answer. These types of problems can be challenging, but with practice, we can become proficient in solving them.

To solve word problems involving expression evaluation, we can follow these steps:

1. Read the problem carefully and identify the key information. Pay attention to any quantities or variables mentioned in the problem.

2. Define variables to represent the unknown quantities. Assign a letter or symbol to each unknown quantity mentioned in the problem. This will help us create an algebraic expression.

3. Write an algebraic expression that represents the given situation. Use the variables defined in the previous step and any mathematical operations necessary to represent the problem.

4. Evaluate the expression by substituting the given values for the variables. Perform the operations in the expression to find the numerical value.

5. Answer the question asked in the problem. Make sure to include units if necessary.

Let's work through an example to see how these steps can be applied.

**Example:**

A store sells T-shirts for $15 each and hats for $10 each. If Sarah buys 3 T-shirts and 2 hats, how much does she spend in total?

**Solution:**

1. Key information: Sarah buys 3 T-shirts and 2 hats.

2. Define variables: Let's use the variable $T$ to represent the number of T-shirts and the variable $H$ to represent the number of hats.

3. Algebraic expression: We can write the expression for the total cost as $15T + 10H$. This represents the cost of the T-shirts ($15T$) plus the cost of the hats ($10H$).

4. Evaluation: Substituting the given values, we have $15(3) + 10(2)$. Evaluating this expression, we get $45 + 20 = 65$.

5. Answer: Sarah spends a total of $65 on T-shirts and hats.

By following these steps, we can solve word problems involving expression evaluation. Remember to carefully read the problem, define variables, write the algebraic expression, evaluate it, and answer the question asked. With practice, you'll become more comfortable and confident in solving these types of problems.

### Section: Evaluating expressions word problems

In the previous section, we learned how to evaluate expressions by substituting specific values for the variables and performing the necessary operations. This allowed us to find the numerical value of the expression based on the given values of the variables. Now, let's apply our knowledge of evaluating expressions to solve word problems.

Word problems involving expression evaluation often require us to translate a real-life situation into an algebraic expression and then evaluate that expression to find the answer. These types of problems can be challenging, but with practice, we can become proficient in solving them.

To solve word problems involving expression evaluation, we can follow these steps:

1. Read the problem carefully and identify the key information. Pay attention to any quantities or variables mentioned in the problem.

2. Define variables to represent the unknown quantities. Assign a letter or symbol to each unknown quantity mentioned in the problem. This will help us create an algebraic expression.

3. Write an algebraic expression that represents the given situation. Use the variables defined in the previous step and any mathematical operations necessary to represent the problem.

4. Evaluate the expression by substituting the given values for the variables. Perform the operations in the expression to find the numerical value.

5. Answer the question asked in the problem. Make sure to include units if necessary.

Let's work through an example to see how these steps can be applied.

**Example:**

A store sells T-shirts for $15 each and hats for $10 each. If Sarah buys 3 T-shirts and 2 hats, how much does she spend in total?

**Solution:**

1. Key information: Sarah buys 3 T-shirts and 2 hats.

2. Define variables: Let's use the variable $T$ to represent the number of T-shirts and the variable $H$ to represent the number of hats.

3. Algebraic expression: We can write the expression for the total cost as follows:

$$
\text{Total cost} = (15 \times T) + (10 \times H)
$$

4. Evaluate the expression: Since Sarah buys 3 T-shirts and 2 hats, we can substitute these values into the expression:

$$
\text{Total cost} = (15 \times 3) + (10 \times 2)
$$

Simplifying the expression, we get:

$$
\text{Total cost} = 45 + 20
$$

$$
\text{Total cost} = 65
$$

5. Answer: Sarah spends a total of $65.

By following these steps, we can solve word problems involving expression evaluation. It's important to practice these types of problems to become more comfortable with the process. In the next section, we will explore some challenging word problems on expression evaluation to further strengthen our skills.

### Section: Writing algebraic expressions introduction

In the previous section, we learned how to evaluate expressions by substituting specific values for the variables and performing the necessary operations. This allowed us to find the numerical value of the expression based on the given values of the variables. Now, let's take a step back and focus on how to write algebraic expressions.

Writing algebraic expressions is an essential skill in mathematics. It allows us to represent real-life situations and mathematical relationships using symbols and variables. By using algebraic expressions, we can solve problems and analyze patterns more efficiently.

To write algebraic expressions, we need to understand the language of mathematics. Just like we use words and sentences to communicate in English, we use symbols and mathematical operations to express mathematical ideas. Let's go over some key concepts to help us understand how to write algebraic expressions.

#### Understanding Variables

In mathematics, a variable is a symbol that represents an unknown quantity or a changing value. Variables are often denoted by letters, such as $x$, $y$, or $z$. We use variables to express relationships between quantities and to solve problems.

For example, let's say we want to represent the length of a rectangle. We can use the variable $l$ to represent the length. If the length of the rectangle is 5 units, we can write the algebraic expression as $l = 5$. Here, $l$ is the variable representing the length, and 5 is the value assigned to that variable.

#### Mathematical Operations

In algebraic expressions, we use mathematical operations to combine variables and constants. The four basic mathematical operations are addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division. These operations allow us to perform calculations and manipulate expressions.

For example, let's say we want to represent the sum of two numbers, $a$ and $b$. We can write the algebraic expression as $a + b$. Here, the plus sign represents addition, and $a$ and $b$ are the variables being added.

#### Translating Real-Life Situations

To write algebraic expressions, we often need to translate real-life situations into mathematical language. This involves identifying the key information, defining variables, and using mathematical operations to represent the problem.

Let's work through an example to see how these steps can be applied.

**Example:**

Suppose you are planning a party and want to determine the total cost of the party supplies. You know that each balloon costs $2, each bag of chips costs $3, and each pizza costs $10. Let's write an algebraic expression to represent the total cost of the party supplies.

1. Key information: Balloons cost $2, bags of chips cost $3, and pizzas cost $10.

2. Define variables: Let's use the variable $B$ to represent the number of balloons, $C$ to represent the number of bags of chips, and $P$ to represent the number of pizzas.

3. Algebraic expression: The total cost can be represented by the expression $2B + 3C + 10P$. Here, we multiply the number of balloons by the cost per balloon ($2B$), the number of bags of chips by the cost per bag ($3C$), and the number of pizzas by the cost per pizza ($10P$). Then, we add these three terms together to get the total cost.

Now that we have learned the basics of writing algebraic expressions, we can move on to understanding how to simplify and solve these expressions. Stay tuned for the next section where we will explore these topics in more detail.

### Section: Writing basic algebraic expressions word problems

In the previous section, we learned how to write algebraic expressions to represent mathematical relationships and real-life situations. Now, let's take our understanding a step further and learn how to write word problems involving algebraic expressions.

Word problems are a common way to apply mathematical concepts to real-world scenarios. They help us develop problem-solving skills and apply mathematical knowledge in practical situations. By writing word problems involving algebraic expressions, we can practice translating verbal descriptions into mathematical equations.

To write word problems involving algebraic expressions, we need to carefully analyze the given information and identify the key elements. Let's go through the steps to solve word problems using algebraic expressions:

1. Read the problem carefully: Start by reading the word problem multiple times to ensure you understand the context and the information provided.

2. Identify the unknowns: Determine the quantities or values that are unknown and need to be represented by variables. Assign a variable to each unknown quantity.

3. Translate the information into expressions: Use the given information to write algebraic expressions that represent the relationships between the known and unknown quantities. Use the appropriate mathematical operations (+, -, ×, ÷) to combine the variables and constants.

4. Write the equation: Once you have translated the information into algebraic expressions, use the expressions to write an equation that represents the problem. The equation should relate the known and unknown quantities.

5. Solve the equation: Solve the equation to find the value of the unknown variable. Use the properties of equality and the rules of algebra to simplify the equation and isolate the variable.

6. Check your solution: After finding the value of the unknown variable, substitute it back into the original problem to ensure that the solution makes sense in the given context.

Let's practice writing a word problem involving algebraic expressions:

**Example:**

A store sells T-shirts for $15 each. If a customer buys $x$ T-shirts, how much will they spend in total?

**Solution:**

1. Read the problem carefully: The problem states that a store sells T-shirts for $15 each, and we need to find out how much a customer will spend if they buy a certain number of T-shirts.

2. Identify the unknowns: The unknown quantity in this problem is the number of T-shirts the customer buys. Let's assign the variable $x$ to represent the number of T-shirts.

3. Translate the information into expressions: The cost of each T-shirt is $15. To find the total cost, we can multiply the cost per T-shirt by the number of T-shirts. The algebraic expression representing the total cost is $15x$.

4. Write the equation: The equation representing the problem is $15x = \text{total cost}$.

5. Solve the equation: To find the total cost, we need to solve the equation $15x = \text{total cost}$. If we know the value of $x$, we can substitute it into the equation to find the total cost.

6. Check your solution: Once we find the value of $x$ and calculate the total cost, we can substitute these values back into the original problem to ensure that our solution is correct.

By following these steps, we can write word problems involving algebraic expressions and solve them to find the unknown quantities. Practice is key to mastering this skill, so make sure to try out different word problems to strengthen your understanding.

### Section: Least common multiple

The least common multiple (LCM) is an important concept in mathematics that helps us find the smallest multiple that two or more numbers have in common. In this section, we will explore the concept of the least common multiple and learn how to find it.

#### Understanding the concept of least common multiple

The least common multiple of two or more numbers is the smallest number that is divisible by each of the given numbers. It is often denoted as LCM.

To understand the concept of LCM, let's consider an example. Suppose we have two numbers, 4 and 6. The multiples of 4 are 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, and so on. The multiples of 6 are 6, 12, 18, 24, 30, and so on. From these lists, we can see that the smallest number that is divisible by both 4 and 6 is 12. Therefore, the LCM of 4 and 6 is 12.

Finding the LCM involves identifying the common multiples of the given numbers and selecting the smallest one. One way to find the LCM is by listing the multiples of each number and finding the common multiples. However, this method can be time-consuming and inefficient, especially for larger numbers.

A more efficient method to find the LCM is by using prime factorization. This method involves breaking down each number into its prime factors and then multiplying the highest power of each prime factor. Let's go through an example to understand this method better.

Example:
Find the LCM of 12 and 18.

Step 1: Prime factorize each number.
- Prime factorization of 12: 2^2 * 3
- Prime factorization of 18: 2 * 3^2

Step 2: Multiply the highest power of each prime factor.
- Highest power of 2: 2^2 = 4
- Highest power of 3: 3^2 = 9

Step 3: Multiply the results from step 2.
- LCM of 12 and 18 = 4 * 9 = 36

Therefore, the LCM of 12 and 18 is 36.

Using prime factorization to find the LCM is a more efficient method, especially for larger numbers. It allows us to find the LCM quickly and accurately.

In the next section, we will explore how to find the LCM of more than two numbers and apply this concept to solve various mathematical problems.

### Section: Least common multiple

The least common multiple (LCM) is an important concept in mathematics that helps us find the smallest multiple that two or more numbers have in common. In this section, we will explore the concept of the least common multiple and learn how to find it.

#### Understanding the concept of least common multiple

The least common multiple of two or more numbers is the smallest number that is divisible by each of the given numbers. It is often denoted as LCM.

To understand the concept of LCM, let's consider an example. Suppose we have two numbers, 4 and 6. The multiples of 4 are 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, and so on. The multiples of 6 are 6, 12, 18, 24, 30, and so on. From these lists, we can see that the smallest number that is divisible by both 4 and 6 is 12. Therefore, the LCM of 4 and 6 is 12.

Finding the LCM involves identifying the common multiples of the given numbers and selecting the smallest one. One way to find the LCM is by listing the multiples of each number and finding the common multiples. However, this method can be time-consuming and inefficient, especially for larger numbers.

A more efficient method to find the LCM is by using prime factorization. This method involves breaking down each number into its prime factors and then multiplying the highest power of each prime factor. Let's go through an example to understand this method better.

Example:
Find the LCM of 12 and 18.

Step 1: Prime factorize each number.
- Prime factorization of 12: $2^2 \times 3$
- Prime factorization of 18: $2 \times 3^2$

Step 2: Multiply the highest power of each prime factor.
- Highest power of 2: $2^2 = 4$
- Highest power of 3: $3^2 = 9$

Step 3: Multiply the results from step 2.
- LCM of 12 and 18 = $4 \times 9 = 36$

Therefore, the LCM of 12 and 18 is 36.

Using prime factorization to find the LCM is a more efficient method, especially for larger numbers. It allows us to find the LCM quickly and accurately.

#### Practice problems on least common multiple

Now that we understand how to find the LCM using prime factorization, let's practice solving some problems.

**Problem 1:** Find the LCM of 8 and 12.

**Solution:** 
Step 1: Prime factorize each number.
- Prime factorization of 8: $2^3$
- Prime factorization of 12: $2^2 \times 3$

Step 2: Multiply the highest power of each prime factor.
- Highest power of 2: $2^3 = 8$
- Highest power of 3: $3^1 = 3$

Step 3: Multiply the results from step 2.
- LCM of 8 and 12 = $8 \times 3 = 24$

Therefore, the LCM of 8 and 12 is 24.

**Problem 2:** Find the LCM of 15 and 20.

**Solution:** 
Step 1: Prime factorize each number.
- Prime factorization of 15: $3 \times 5$
- Prime factorization of 20: $2^2 \times 5$

Step 2: Multiply the highest power of each prime factor.
- Highest power of 2: $2^2 = 4$
- Highest power of 3: $3^1 = 3$
- Highest power of 5: $5^1 = 5$

Step 3: Multiply the results from step 2.
- LCM of 15 and 20 = $4 \times 3 \times 5 = 60$

Therefore, the LCM of 15 and 20 is 60.

Continue practicing solving problems on finding the LCM using prime factorization. Remember to break down each number into its prime factors and multiply the highest power of each prime factor to find the LCM.

### Section: Greatest common factor

In the previous section, we learned about the concept of the least common multiple (LCM) and how to find it. Now, let's shift our focus to another important concept in mathematics: the greatest common factor (GCF).

The greatest common factor is the largest number that divides evenly into two or more given numbers. It is often denoted as GCF. Finding the GCF is useful in simplifying fractions, factoring polynomials, and solving equations.

To understand the concept of GCF, let's consider an example. Suppose we have two numbers, 12 and 18. The factors of 12 are 1, 2, 3, 4, 6, and 12. The factors of 18 are 1, 2, 3, 6, 9, and 18. From these lists, we can see that the largest number that divides evenly into both 12 and 18 is 6. Therefore, the GCF of 12 and 18 is 6.

Finding the GCF involves identifying the common factors of the given numbers and selecting the largest one. One way to find the GCF is by listing the factors of each number and finding the common factors. However, this method can be time-consuming and inefficient, especially for larger numbers.

A more efficient method to find the GCF is by using prime factorization, similar to how we found the LCM. This method involves breaking down each number into its prime factors and then selecting the common prime factors with the lowest exponent. Let's go through an example to understand this method better.

Example:
Find the GCF of 24 and 36.

Step 1: Prime factorize each number.
- Prime factorization of 24: $2^3 \times 3$
- Prime factorization of 36: $2^2 \times 3^2$

Step 2: Select the common prime factors with the lowest exponent.
- Common prime factor: $2^2 = 4$
- Common prime factor: $3^1 = 3$

Step 3: Multiply the results from step 2.
- GCF of 24 and 36 = $4 \times 3 = 12$

Therefore, the GCF of 24 and 36 is 12.

Using prime factorization to find the GCF is a more efficient method, especially for larger numbers. It allows us to find the GCF quickly and accurately.

Now that we understand the concept of the greatest common factor, let's practice solving some problems in the next section.

### Section: Greatest common factor

In the previous section, we learned about the concept of the least common multiple (LCM) and how to find it. Now, let's shift our focus to another important concept in mathematics: the greatest common factor (GCF).

The greatest common factor is the largest number that divides evenly into two or more given numbers. It is often denoted as GCF. Finding the GCF is useful in simplifying fractions, factoring polynomials, and solving equations.

To understand the concept of GCF, let's consider an example. Suppose we have two numbers, 12 and 18. The factors of 12 are 1, 2, 3, 4, 6, and 12. The factors of 18 are 1, 2, 3, 6, 9, and 18. From these lists, we can see that the largest number that divides evenly into both 12 and 18 is 6. Therefore, the GCF of 12 and 18 is 6.

Finding the GCF involves identifying the common factors of the given numbers and selecting the largest one. One way to find the GCF is by listing the factors of each number and finding the common factors. However, this method can be time-consuming and inefficient, especially for larger numbers.

A more efficient method to find the GCF is by using prime factorization, similar to how we found the LCM. This method involves breaking down each number into its prime factors and then selecting the common prime factors with the lowest exponent. Let's go through an example to understand this method better.

Example:
Find the GCF of 24 and 36.

Step 1: Prime factorize each number.
- Prime factorization of 24: $2^3 \times 3$
- Prime factorization of 36: $2^2 \times 3^2$

Step 2: Select the common prime factors with the lowest exponent.
- Common prime factor: $2^2 = 4$
- Common prime factor: $3^1 = 3$

Step 3: Multiply the results from step 2.
- GCF of 24 and 36 = $4 \times 3 = 12$

Therefore, the GCF of 24 and 36 is 12.

Using prime factorization to find the GCF is a more efficient method, especially for larger numbers. It allows us to find the GCF quickly and accurately.

Now that we have learned how to find the GCF using prime factorization, let's practice solving some problems on finding the GCF.

3. Find the GCF of 45 and 75.

4. Find the GCF of 72 and 90.

1. GCF of 16 and 24 = 8
2. GCF of 36 and 48 = 12

Great job! You have successfully practiced finding the GCF using prime factorization. Keep practicing to strengthen your understanding of this important concept in mathematics.

### Section: Distributive property with variables

In the previous section, we learned about the concept of the greatest common factor (GCF) and how to find it. Now, let's shift our focus to another important topic in mathematics: the distributive property with variables.

The distributive property is a fundamental concept in algebra that allows us to simplify expressions and solve equations. It states that when we multiply a number or variable by a sum or difference inside parentheses, we can distribute the multiplication to each term inside the parentheses.

For example, let's consider the expression $3(x + 2)$. To simplify this expression using the distributive property, we multiply 3 by each term inside the parentheses:

$$3(x + 2) = 3 \cdot x + 3 \cdot 2$$

This simplifies to:

$$3x + 6$$

By distributing the multiplication, we have simplified the expression.

Now, let's explore how the distributive property works with variables. Consider the expression $2a(b + c)$. To simplify this expression, we distribute the multiplication of 2a to each term inside the parentheses:

$$2a(b + c) = 2a \cdot b + 2a \cdot c$$

This simplifies to:

$$2ab + 2ac$$

By distributing the multiplication, we have simplified the expression.

The distributive property is a powerful tool that allows us to simplify complex expressions and solve equations. It is especially useful when dealing with variables and expressions involving multiple terms. By understanding and applying the distributive property, we can manipulate expressions and equations to make them easier to work with.

Now that we have a basic understanding of the distributive property with variables, let's dive deeper into this topic and explore some examples to solidify our understanding.

### Section: Distributive property with variables

In the previous section, we learned about the concept of the greatest common factor (GCF) and how to find it. Now, let's shift our focus to another important topic in mathematics: the distributive property with variables.

This simplifies to:

$$3x + 6$$

By distributing the multiplication, we have simplified the expression.

$$2a(b + c) = 2a \cdot b + 2a \cdot c$$

This simplifies to:

$$2ab + 2ac$$

### Section: Combining like terms

In the previous section, we learned about the distributive property with variables, which allows us to simplify expressions and solve equations. Now, let's shift our focus to another important concept in algebra: combining like terms.

When we have an expression with multiple terms, we can simplify it by combining terms that have the same variable and exponent. These terms are called "like terms." By combining like terms, we can simplify the expression and make it easier to work with.

To understand how to combine like terms, let's consider an example. Suppose we have the expression $3x + 2x$. Both terms have the variable $x$ raised to the power of 1, so they are like terms. To combine them, we add their coefficients:

$$3x + 2x = (3 + 2)x = 5x$$

By combining the like terms $3x$ and $2x$, we simplified the expression to $5x$.

Now, let's look at an example with variables raised to different exponents. Suppose we have the expression $2x^2 + 3x^2$. Although the exponents are different, the terms still have the same variable $x$. Therefore, they are like terms. To combine them, we add their coefficients:

$$2x^2 + 3x^2 = (2 + 3)x^2 = 5x^2$$

By combining the like terms $2x^2$ and $3x^2$, we simplified the expression to $5x^2$.

It's important to note that we can only combine terms that have the same variable and exponent. For example, we cannot combine $3x$ and $2y$ because they have different variables. Similarly, we cannot combine $2x^2$ and $3x$ because they have different exponents.

By combining like terms, we can simplify expressions and make them easier to work with. This skill is essential in algebra and will be used extensively as we progress through more advanced topics. Now, let's practice combining like terms in some example problems.

### Section: Combining like terms

In the previous section, we learned about the distributive property with variables, which allows us to simplify expressions and solve equations. Now, let's shift our focus to another important concept in algebra: combining like terms.

When we have an expression with multiple terms, we can simplify it by combining terms that have the same variable and exponent. These terms are called "like terms." By combining like terms, we can simplify the expression and make it easier to work with.

To understand how to combine like terms, let's consider an example. Suppose we have the expression $3x + 2x$. Both terms have the variable $x$ raised to the power of 1, so they are like terms. To combine them, we add their coefficients:

$$3x + 2x = (3 + 2)x = 5x$$

By combining the like terms $3x$ and $2x$, we simplified the expression to $5x$.

Now, let's look at an example with variables raised to different exponents. Suppose we have the expression $2x^2 + 3x^2$. Although the exponents are different, the terms still have the same variable $x$. Therefore, they are like terms. To combine them, we add their coefficients:

$$2x^2 + 3x^2 = (2 + 3)x^2 = 5x^2$$

By combining the like terms $2x^2$ and $3x^2$, we simplified the expression to $5x^2$.

It's important to note that we can only combine terms that have the same variable and exponent. For example, we cannot combine $3x$ and $2y$ because they have different variables. Similarly, we cannot combine $2x^2$ and $3x$ because they have different exponents.

By combining like terms, we can simplify expressions and make them easier to work with. This skill is essential in algebra and will be used extensively as we progress through more advanced topics.

#### Practice problems on combining like terms

Now, let's practice combining like terms in some example problems. Remember to look for terms that have the same variable and exponent.

**Problem 1:**

Combine the like terms in the following expression: $4x + 2x - 3x$

**Solution:**

To combine the like terms, we add their coefficients:

$4x + 2x - 3x = (4 + 2 - 3)x = 3x$

Therefore, the simplified expression is $3x$.

**Problem 2:**

Combine the like terms in the following expression: $5y^2 - 2y^2 + 3y^2$

**Solution:**

To combine the like terms, we add their coefficients:

$5y^2 - 2y^2 + 3y^2 = (5 - 2 + 3)y^2 = 6y^2$

Therefore, the simplified expression is $6y^2$.

**Problem 3:**

Combine the like terms in the following expression: $2a + 3b - 4a + 5b$

**Solution:**

Since the terms have different variables, we cannot combine them. Therefore, the expression remains as it is: $2a + 3b - 4a + 5b$.

Remember to always look for like terms and combine them to simplify expressions. This skill will be useful in solving equations and working with more complex algebraic expressions. Keep practicing and you'll become a master of combining like terms in no time!

### Section: Equivalent expressions

In the previous section, we learned about combining like terms to simplify expressions. Now, let's explore another important concept in algebra: equivalent expressions.

Equivalent expressions are expressions that have the same value for all possible values of the variables. In other words, they represent the same mathematical relationship, even though they may look different.

To understand equivalent expressions, let's consider an example. Suppose we have the expression $2x + 3$. This expression represents a relationship between a variable $x$ and the numbers 2 and 3. Now, let's consider another expression $6 + x$. Although this expression looks different, it represents the same relationship between $x$, 2, and 3. Therefore, these two expressions are equivalent.

We can prove that two expressions are equivalent by substituting different values for the variable and checking if the expressions give the same result. For example, if we substitute $x = 4$ into both expressions, we get:

$2(4) + 3 = 8 + 3 = 11$

$6 + 4 = 10$

Since the two expressions give different results for $x = 4$, they are not equivalent.

Now, let's look at an example where two expressions are equivalent. Suppose we have the expressions $3x + 2$ and $2 + 3x$. To prove that these expressions are equivalent, we can substitute different values for $x$ and check if the results are the same. Let's substitute $x = 2$:

$3(2) + 2 = 6 + 2 = 8$

$2 + 3(2) = 2 + 6 = 8$

Since both expressions give the same result for $x = 2$, we can conclude that they are equivalent.

It's important to note that we can use properties of arithmetic and algebra to manipulate expressions and create equivalent expressions. For example, we can use the commutative property of addition to rearrange the terms in an expression without changing its value. This allows us to create equivalent expressions.

Understanding equivalent expressions is crucial in algebra because it allows us to simplify expressions, solve equations, and make connections between different mathematical representations. As we progress through more advanced topics, we will encounter more complex expressions and equations, and the ability to identify and work with equivalent expressions will become even more important.

#### Practice problems on equivalent expressions

Now, let's practice identifying equivalent expressions in some example problems. Remember to substitute different values for the variables and check if the results are the same.

**Problem 1:**

Are the expressions $4x + 2$ and $2(2x + 1)$ equivalent? Explain your answer.

**Problem 2:**

Are the expressions $3x - 5$ and $5 - 3x$ equivalent? Explain your answer.

**Problem 3:**

Are the expressions $2x^2 + 3x$ and $3x + 2x^2$ equivalent? Explain your answer.

By practicing with equivalent expressions, we can strengthen our understanding of algebraic concepts and develop problem-solving skills that will be useful in various mathematical situations.

### Section: Equivalent expressions

Understanding equivalent expressions is crucial in algebra because it allows us to simplify expressions and solve equations more easily. By recognizing equivalent expressions, we can transform complex expressions into simpler forms, making them easier to work with.

#### Practice problems on equivalent expressions

Now, let's practice identifying and creating equivalent expressions. Try solving the following problems:

1. Are the expressions $4x + 2$ and $2 + 4x$ equivalent? Explain your answer.

2. Determine if the expressions $5y - 3$ and $-3 + 5y$ are equivalent. Show your work.

3. Create an equivalent expression for $2(x + 3)$.

Take your time to solve these problems and check your answers. Remember to substitute different values for the variables to verify if the expressions are equivalent.

### Section: Algebraic equations basics

In this section, we will dive into the basics of algebraic equations. Algebraic equations are mathematical statements that use variables and symbols to represent relationships between quantities. They are an essential tool in solving problems and understanding mathematical concepts.

#### What is an algebraic equation?

An algebraic equation is a statement that asserts the equality of two expressions. It consists of two sides, the left-hand side (LHS) and the right-hand side (RHS), separated by an equal sign (=). The LHS and RHS can contain variables, constants, and mathematical operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division.

Let's look at an example of an algebraic equation:

$$2x + 5 = 13$$

In this equation, the variable $x$ represents an unknown value. Our goal is to find the value of $x$ that satisfies the equation. By solving the equation, we can determine the value of $x$ that makes the equation true.

#### Solving algebraic equations

To solve an algebraic equation, we want to isolate the variable on one side of the equation. This is done by performing the same operation on both sides of the equation, maintaining the equality.

Let's solve the previous example equation step by step:

$$2x + 5 = 13$$

First, we want to get rid of the constant term on the left-hand side. We can do this by subtracting 5 from both sides of the equation:

$$2x + 5 - 5 = 13 - 5$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$2x = 8$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $x$. Since $2x$ means $2$ multiplied by $x$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $2$:

$$\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{8}{2}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$x = 4$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $2x + 5 = 13$ is $x = 4$.

#### Checking solutions

After finding a solution to an algebraic equation, it is important to check if the solution is valid. We can do this by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation and verifying if both sides are equal.

Let's check if $x = 4$ is a valid solution for the equation $2x + 5 = 13$:

$$2(4) + 5 = 13$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$8 + 5 = 13$$

$$13 = 13$$

Since both sides of the equation are equal, we can conclude that $x = 4$ is indeed a valid solution.

#### Summary

In this section, we learned about algebraic equations and how to solve them. Algebraic equations are mathematical statements that use variables and symbols to represent relationships between quantities. To solve an algebraic equation, we isolate the variable on one side of the equation by performing the same operation on both sides, maintaining the equality. It is important to check the solution by substituting it back into the original equation.

In the next section, we will explore different types of algebraic equations and learn more advanced techniques for solving them.

### Section: Algebraic equations basics

In this section, we will continue our exploration of algebraic equations. We have already learned that algebraic equations are mathematical statements that use variables and symbols to represent relationships between quantities. They are an essential tool in solving problems and understanding mathematical concepts. Now, let's dive deeper into the topic and practice solving some algebraic equations.

#### Practice problems on algebraic equations

To become proficient in solving algebraic equations, it is important to practice. Let's work through a few practice problems together:

##### Problem 1:

Solve the equation: $3x + 7 = 22$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $x$ on one side. Let's start by subtracting 7 from both sides of the equation:

$$3x + 7 - 7 = 22 - 7$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$3x = 15$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $x$. Since $3x$ means $3$ multiplied by $x$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $3$:

$$\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{15}{3}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$x = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $3x + 7 = 22$ is $x = 5$.

##### Problem 2:

Solve the equation: $2y - 4 = 10$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $y$ on one side. Let's start by adding 4 to both sides of the equation:

$$2y - 4 + 4 = 10 + 4$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$2y = 14$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $y$. Since $2y$ means $2$ multiplied by $y$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $2$:

$$\frac{2y}{2} = \frac{14}{2}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$y = 7$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $2y - 4 = 10$ is $y = 7$.

##### Problem 3:

Solve the equation: $4z + 9 = 37$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $z$ on one side. Let's start by subtracting 9 from both sides of the equation:

$$4z + 9 - 9 = 37 - 9$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$4z = 28$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $z$. Since $4z$ means $4$ multiplied by $z$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $4$:

$$\frac{4z}{4} = \frac{28}{4}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$z = 7$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $4z + 9 = 37$ is $z = 7$.

By practicing these problems, you are building your skills in solving algebraic equations. Remember to always check your solutions by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation and verifying if both sides are equal.

In the next section, we will explore inequalities and how they relate to equations. Stay tuned for more exciting math concepts!

### Section: One-step equations intuition

In the previous section, we explored the basics of algebraic equations and practiced solving some examples. Now, let's develop our intuition for solving one-step equations.

#### Understanding one-step equations

One-step equations are mathematical statements that involve only one operation to solve for the unknown variable. These equations can be solved by performing a single operation, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division, to isolate the variable.

To better understand one-step equations, let's consider an example:

**Example 1:**

Solve the equation: $2x + 5 = 17$

In this equation, we want to find the value of $x$ that makes the equation true. To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of the term that is connected to $x$. In this case, the term connected to $x$ is $2x$, and it is being added to 5.

To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of addition, which is subtraction. By subtracting 5 from both sides of the equation, we can eliminate the 5 on the left side:

$$2x + 5 - 5 = 17 - 5$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$2x = 12$$

Next, we want to isolate $x$. Since $2x$ means $2$ multiplied by $x$, we can divide both sides of the equation by 2:

$$\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{12}{2}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$x = 6$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $2x + 5 = 17$ is $x = 6$.

#### Practice problems on one-step equations

To become proficient in solving one-step equations, it is important to practice. Let's work through a few practice problems together:

##### Problem 1:

Solve the equation: $3y - 8 = 19$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $y$ on one side. Let's start by adding 8 to both sides of the equation:

$$3y - 8 + 8 = 19 + 8$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$3y = 27$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $y$. Since $3y$ means $3$ multiplied by $y$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $3$:

$$\frac{3y}{3} = \frac{27}{3}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$y = 9$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $3y - 8 = 19$ is $y = 9$.

##### Problem 2:

Solve the equation: $4z + 7 = 31$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $z$ on one side. Let's start by subtracting 7 from both sides of the equation:

$$4z + 7 - 7 = 31 - 7$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$4z = 24$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $z$. Since $4z$ means $4$ multiplied by $z$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $4$:

$$\frac{4z}{4} = \frac{24}{4}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$z = 6$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $4z + 7 = 31$ is $z = 6$.

By practicing more examples, you will develop a strong intuition for solving one-step equations. Remember to always perform the inverse operation to isolate the variable and find the solution.

### Section: One-step addition & subtraction equations

In the previous section, we explored the basics of algebraic equations and practiced solving some examples. Now, let's dive deeper into solving one-step addition and subtraction equations.

#### Solving one-step addition and subtraction equations

One-step addition and subtraction equations are mathematical statements that involve only one operation of addition or subtraction to solve for the unknown variable. These equations can be solved by performing a single operation to isolate the variable.

To better understand how to solve one-step addition and subtraction equations, let's consider an example:

**Example 1:**

Solve the equation: $4x + 7 = 23$

In this equation, we want to find the value of $x$ that makes the equation true. To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of the term that is connected to $x$. In this case, the term connected to $x$ is $4x$, and it is being added to 7.

To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of addition, which is subtraction. By subtracting 7 from both sides of the equation, we can eliminate the 7 on the left side:

$$4x + 7 - 7 = 23 - 7$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$4x = 16$$

Next, we want to isolate $x$. Since $4x$ means $4$ multiplied by $x$, we can divide both sides of the equation by 4:

$$\frac{4x}{4} = \frac{16}{4}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$x = 4$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $4x + 7 = 23$ is $x = 4$.

Now, let's practice solving some more one-step addition and subtraction equations together.

#### Practice problems on one-step addition and subtraction equations

##### Problem 1:

Solve the equation: $2y - 5 = 11$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $y$ on one side. Let's start by adding 5 to both sides of the equation:

$$2y - 5 + 5 = 11 + 5$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$2y = 16$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $y$. Since $2y$ means $2$ multiplied by $y$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $2$:

$$\frac{2y}{2} = \frac{16}{2}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$y = 8$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $2y - 5 = 11$ is $y = 8$.

##### Problem 2:

Solve the equation: $3z + 9 = 24$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $z$ on one side. Let's start by subtracting 9 from both sides of the equation:

$$3z + 9 - 9 = 24 - 9$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$3z = 15$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $z$. Since $3z$ means $3$ multiplied by $z$, we can divide both sides of the equation by $3$:

$$\frac{3z}{3} = \frac{15}{3}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$z = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $3z + 9 = 24$ is $z = 5$.

By practicing more problems like these, you will become proficient in solving one-step addition and subtraction equations. Keep practicing and you'll master this concept in no time!

In the next section, we will explore one-step multiplication and division equations. Stay tuned!

##### Problem 2:

Solve the equation: $3z + 9 = 24$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $z$ on one side. Let's start by subtracting 9 from both sides of the equation:

$$3z + 9 - 9 = 24 - 9$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$3z = 15$$

Next, we want to isolate the variable $z$. Since $3z$ means $3$ multiplied by $z$, we can divide both sides of the equation by 3:

$$\frac{3z}{3} = \frac{15}{3}$$

Simplifying further, we get:

$$z = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $3z + 9 = 24$ is $z = 5$.

Now, you can try solving the following problems on your own.

##### Problem 3:

Solve the equation: $6a - 4 = 26$

##### Problem 4:

Solve the equation: $8b + 12 = 52$

Remember to isolate the variable by performing the inverse operation and simplify the equation to find the solution.

Keep practicing, and soon you'll be a master of one-step addition and subtraction equations!

### Section: One-step multiplication and division equations

In the previous section, we learned how to solve one-step addition and subtraction equations. Now, let's move on to another important type of equation: one-step multiplication and division equations.

#### Solving one-step multiplication and division equations

One-step multiplication and division equations involve only one operation of multiplication or division to solve for the unknown variable. These equations can be solved by performing a single operation to isolate the variable, just like we did with addition and subtraction equations.

To better understand how to solve one-step multiplication and division equations, let's consider an example:

**Example 1:**

Solve the equation: $3x = 15$

In this equation, we want to find the value of $x$ that makes the equation true. To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of the term that is connected to $x$. In this case, the term connected to $x$ is $3x$, and it is being multiplied by 3.

To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of multiplication, which is division. By dividing both sides of the equation by 3, we can eliminate the 3 on the left side:

$$\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{15}{3}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$x = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $3x = 15$ is $x = 5$.

Now, let's practice solving some more one-step multiplication and division equations together.

#### Practice problems on one-step multiplication and division equations

##### Problem 1:

Solve the equation: $2y = 10$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $y$ on one side. Let's start by dividing both sides of the equation by 2:

$$\frac{2y}{2} = \frac{10}{2}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$y = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $2y = 10$ is $y = 5$.

##### Problem 2:

Solve the equation: $6z = 36$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $z$ on one side. Let's start by dividing both sides of the equation by 6:

$$\frac{6z}{6} = \frac{36}{6}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$z = 6$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $6z = 36$ is $z = 6$.

Now, it's your turn to practice solving some one-step multiplication and division equations. Remember to always perform the inverse operation to isolate the variable and find the solution.

### Section: One-step multiplication and division equations

In the previous section, we learned how to solve one-step addition and subtraction equations. Now, let's move on to another important type of equation: one-step multiplication and division equations.

#### Solving one-step multiplication and division equations

One-step multiplication and division equations involve only one operation of multiplication or division to solve for the unknown variable. These equations can be solved by performing a single operation to isolate the variable, just like we did with addition and subtraction equations.

To better understand how to solve one-step multiplication and division equations, let's consider an example:

**Example 1:**

Solve the equation: $3x = 15$

In this equation, we want to find the value of $x$ that makes the equation true. To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of the term that is connected to $x$. In this case, the term connected to $x$ is $3x$, and it is being multiplied by 3.

To isolate $x$, we need to perform the inverse operation of multiplication, which is division. By dividing both sides of the equation by 3, we can eliminate the 3 on the left side:

$$\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{15}{3}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$x = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $3x = 15$ is $x = 5$.

Now, let's practice solving some more one-step multiplication and division equations together.

#### Practice problems on one-step multiplication and division equations

##### Problem 1:

Solve the equation: $2y = 10$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $y$ on one side. Let's start by dividing both sides of the equation by 2:

$$\frac{2y}{2} = \frac{10}{2}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$y = 5$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $2y = 10$ is $y = 5$.

##### Problem 2:

Solve the equation: $6z = 36$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $z$ on one side. Let's start by dividing both sides of the equation by 6:

$$\frac{6z}{6} = \frac{36}{6}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$z = 6$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $6z = 36$ is $z = 6$.

#### Challenging problems on one-step multiplication and division equations

Now that we have practiced solving some basic one-step multiplication and division equations, let's move on to some challenging problems. These problems will require a deeper understanding of the concepts and may involve multiple steps to solve.

##### Problem 3:

Solve the equation: $4x = 12 + 2x$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $x$ on one side. Let's start by subtracting $2x$ from both sides of the equation:

$$4x - 2x = 12 + 2x - 2x$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$2x = 12$$

Next, let's divide both sides of the equation by 2 to isolate $x$:

$$\frac{2x}{2} = \frac{12}{2}$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$x = 6$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $4x = 12 + 2x$ is $x = 6$.

##### Problem 4:

Solve the equation: $\frac{3}{x} = \frac{1}{4}$

To solve this equation, we want to isolate the variable $x$ on one side. Let's start by multiplying both sides of the equation by $x$:

$$\frac{3}{x} \cdot x = \frac{1}{4} \cdot x$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$3 = \frac{x}{4}$$

Next, let's multiply both sides of the equation by 4 to eliminate the fraction:

$$3 \cdot 4 = \frac{x}{4} \cdot 4$$

Simplifying, we have:

$$12 = x$$

Therefore, the solution to the equation $\frac{3}{x} = \frac{1}{4}$ is $x = 12$.

By practicing these challenging problems, you will become more confident in solving one-step multiplication and division equations. Keep practicing and you'll master this topic in no time!

### Next section: Two-step equations

### Section: Finding mistakes in one-step equations

In the previous section, we learned how to solve one-step multiplication and division equations. Now, let's focus on a different aspect of working with equations - finding mistakes in one-step equations. 

#### Identifying mistakes in one-step equations

When solving equations, it's important to be careful and avoid making mistakes. Mistakes can happen at any step of the process, and it's crucial to be able to identify and correct them. Let's look at some common mistakes that students make when solving one-step equations and how to spot them.

**Mistake 1: Incorrectly applying the inverse operation**

One common mistake is applying the wrong inverse operation when isolating the variable. For example, instead of dividing, a student might mistakenly multiply both sides of the equation. This mistake can lead to an incorrect solution.

To identify this mistake, carefully check the steps taken to isolate the variable. Make sure that the inverse operation is correctly applied. If you notice that the operation used does not match the original equation, then there might be an error.

**Mistake 2: Errors in arithmetic calculations**

Another common mistake is making errors in arithmetic calculations. This can happen when performing operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division. Even a small mistake in calculation can lead to an incorrect solution.

To identify this mistake, double-check all the calculations made during the solution process. Pay attention to each step and ensure that the arithmetic is done accurately. If you find any inconsistencies or errors in the calculations, then there might be a mistake.

**Mistake 3: Misinterpreting the equation**

Sometimes, students misinterpret the equation itself, leading to incorrect solutions. They might misunderstand the operation or the relationship between the terms in the equation. This can result in solving for the wrong variable or using the wrong operation.

To identify this mistake, carefully read and understand the given equation. Make sure you correctly identify the operation and the variable you need to solve for. If you notice any inconsistencies or confusion in the interpretation of the equation, then there might be a mistake.

#### Practice problems on identifying mistakes in one-step equations

Now, let's practice identifying mistakes in one-step equations. Take a look at the following examples and see if you can spot the errors:

##### Problem 1:

Find the mistake in the solution of the equation: $4x = 16$

**Incorrect Solution:**

$$4x = 16$$
$$x = 4$$

**Explanation:**

The mistake in this solution is that the student divided both sides of the equation by 4 instead of multiplying. To solve the equation $4x = 16$, we need to isolate $x$ by performing the inverse operation, which is division. The correct solution should be:

$$4x = 16$$
$$\frac{4x}{4} = \frac{16}{4}$$
$$x = 4$$

##### Problem 2:

Find the mistake in the solution of the equation: $7y = 35$

**Incorrect Solution:**

$$7y = 35$$
$$y = 7$$

**Explanation:**

The mistake in this solution is that the student divided both sides of the equation by 7 instead of multiplying. To solve the equation $7y = 35$, we need to isolate $y$ by performing the inverse operation, which is division. The correct solution should be:

$$7y = 35$$
$$\frac{7y}{7} = \frac{35}{7}$$
$$y = 5$$

By practicing identifying mistakes in one-step equations, you will become more confident in your problem-solving skills and avoid common errors. Remember to carefully analyze each step and check for any inconsistencies or errors.

### Section: Finding mistakes in one-step equations

### Subsection: Practice problems on finding mistakes in one-step equations

Now that we have learned about the common mistakes in one-step equations, let's practice identifying and correcting these mistakes. Solving practice problems will help reinforce our understanding and improve our ability to spot errors.

#### Problem 1:

Solve the following equation for the variable $x$:

$$
3x + 5 = 20
$$

Solution:

Step 1: Subtract 5 from both sides of the equation.

$$
3x + 5 - 5 = 20 - 5
$$

Step 2: Simplify both sides of the equation.

$$
3x = 15
$$

Step 3: Divide both sides of the equation by 3.

$$
\frac{3x}{3} = \frac{15}{3}
$$

Step 4: Simplify both sides of the equation.

$$
x = 5
$$

In this problem, there are no mistakes. The solution is correct.

#### Problem 2:

Solve the following equation for the variable $y$:

$$
\frac{2}{3}y - 4 = 8
$$

Solution:

Step 1: Add 4 to both sides of the equation.

$$
\frac{2}{3}y - 4 + 4 = 8 + 4
$$

Step 2: Simplify both sides of the equation.

$$
\frac{2}{3}y = 12
$$

Step 3: Multiply both sides of the equation by $\frac{3}{2}$.

$$
\frac{2}{3}y \cdot \frac{3}{2} = 12 \cdot \frac{3}{2}
$$

Step 4: Simplify both sides of the equation.

$$
y = 18
$$

In this problem, there are no mistakes. The solution is correct.

#### Problem 3:

Solve the following equation for the variable $z$:

$$
5z - 3 = 12
$$

Solution:

Step 1: Add 3 to both sides of the equation.

$$
5z - 3 + 3 = 12 + 3
$$

Step 2: Simplify both sides of the equation.

$$
5z = 15
$$

Step 3: Divide both sides of the equation by 5.

$$
\frac{5z}{5} = \frac{15}{5}
$$

Step 4: Simplify both sides of the equation.

$$
z = 3
$$

In this problem, there are no mistakes. The solution is correct.

By practicing solving equations and identifying mistakes, we can become more confident in our ability to solve one-step equations accurately. Remember to carefully check each step and ensure that the inverse operation is correctly applied. Also, double-check all arithmetic calculations to avoid errors. With practice, we can master the skill of finding mistakes in one-step equations.

### Section: One-step equation word problems

In the previous section, we learned how to find mistakes in one-step equations. Now, let's move on to solving word problems involving one-step equations. Word problems are a common way to apply mathematical concepts to real-life situations. By solving word problems, we can practice using equations to find unknown values.

#### Solving word problems involving one-step equations

Word problems involving one-step equations can be solved by following a few simple steps. Let's go through the process together:

Step 1: Read the problem carefully and identify the unknown value. The unknown value is the quantity we are trying to find.

Step 2: Define a variable to represent the unknown value. Choose a letter or symbol that makes sense in the context of the problem.

Step 3: Write an equation that represents the relationship between the known values and the unknown value. Use the given information in the problem to set up the equation.

Step 4: Solve the equation by performing the inverse operation to isolate the variable. Remember to perform the same operation on both sides of the equation to maintain balance.

Step 5: Check your solution by substituting the value of the variable back into the original equation. Make sure both sides of the equation are equal.

Let's work through an example to illustrate these steps:

**Example:**

A store is having a sale where all items are 20% off. If the original price of a shirt is $25, what is the sale price?

Step 1: The unknown value is the sale price of the shirt.

Step 2: Let's use the variable $s$ to represent the sale price.

Step 3: The original price of the shirt is $25. Since the sale price is 20% off, we can write the equation: $s = 25 - 0.2 \cdot 25$.

Step 4: To solve the equation, we need to perform the inverse operation. In this case, we need to subtract 20% of the original price from the original price. Simplifying the equation, we have $s = 25 - 5$.

Step 5: The solution is $s = 20$. To check our solution, we substitute $s = 20$ back into the original equation: $20 = 25 - 5$. Both sides of the equation are equal, so our solution is correct.

In this example, the sale price of the shirt is $20.

By practicing solving word problems involving one-step equations, you will become more comfortable with using equations to solve real-life situations. Remember to carefully read the problem, define variables, set up equations, and solve for the unknown value.

### Section: One-step equation word problems

### Section: Intro to inequalities with variables

In the previous section, we learned about solving word problems involving one-step equations. Now, let's move on to a new topic: inequalities with variables. 

An inequality is a mathematical statement that compares two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). Inequalities are used to represent relationships between numbers that are not necessarily equal.

Inequalities with variables are similar to equations with variables, but instead of finding a specific value for the variable, we are looking for a range of values that satisfy the inequality. This range of values is called the solution set.

To understand inequalities with variables, let's go through an example:

**Example:**

Suppose we have the inequality $2x + 3 > 7$. We want to find the range of values for $x$ that make this inequality true.

To solve this inequality, we follow similar steps as we do for equations:

Step 1: Simplify the inequality if necessary. In this case, we can subtract 3 from both sides to get $2x > 4$.

Step 2: Divide both sides of the inequality by 2 to isolate the variable. We get $x > 2$.

Step 3: The solution to this inequality is all values of $x$ that are greater than 2. We can represent this solution set using interval notation as $(2, \infty)$.

It's important to note that when we multiply or divide both sides of an inequality by a negative number, we need to reverse the direction of the inequality symbol. For example, if we have $-3x < 9$, dividing both sides by -3 gives us $x > -3$.

Inequalities with variables can also involve more than one variable. In these cases, we can graph the solution set on a coordinate plane to visualize the range of values that satisfy the inequality.

In the next section, we will explore different types of inequalities and learn how to solve them. Stay tuned!

### Section: Intro to inequalities with variables

In the previous section, we learned about solving word problems involving one-step equations. Now, let's move on to a new topic: inequalities with variables. 

An inequality is a mathematical statement that compares two quantities using symbols such as < (less than), > (greater than), ≤ (less than or equal to), or ≥ (greater than or equal to). Inequalities are used to represent relationships between numbers that are not necessarily equal.

### Section: Dependent and independent variables

In the previous section, we learned about solving inequalities with variables. Now, let's dive deeper into the concept of variables and explore the relationship between dependent and independent variables.

In mathematics, a variable is a symbol that represents an unknown value. It can take on different values, and its value can change depending on other factors. Variables are used to express relationships between quantities and are an essential part of equations and inequalities.

When working with equations and inequalities, it's important to understand the distinction between dependent and independent variables.

#### Understanding dependent and independent variables

A **dependent variable** is a variable that depends on another variable. Its value is determined by the value of the independent variable. In other words, the dependent variable "depends" on the independent variable.

On the other hand, an **independent variable** is a variable that can be freely chosen or manipulated. Its value does not depend on any other variable in the equation or inequality.

To better understand this concept, let's go through an example:

**Example:**

Suppose we have the equation $y = 2x + 3$. In this equation, $y$ is the dependent variable, and $x$ is the independent variable.

The equation tells us that the value of $y$ depends on the value of $x$. If we choose a specific value for $x$, we can calculate the corresponding value of $y$ using the equation. For example, if we set $x$ to 2, we can find $y$ by substituting $x = 2$ into the equation:

$$y = 2(2) + 3 = 7$$

In this case, $y$ is dependent on the value of $x$. If we change the value of $x$, the value of $y$ will also change accordingly.

Understanding the relationship between dependent and independent variables is crucial in solving equations and inequalities. By identifying the dependent and independent variables in a problem, we can determine the nature of the relationship between them and find solutions that satisfy the given conditions.

In the next section, we will explore more examples and practice problems to solidify our understanding of dependent and independent variables.

### Section: Dependent and independent variables

In the previous section, we learned about solving inequalities with variables. Now, let's dive deeper into the concept of variables and explore the relationship between dependent and independent variables.

In mathematics, a variable is a symbol that represents an unknown value. It can take on different values, and its value can change depending on other factors. Variables are used to express relationships between quantities and are an essential part of equations and inequalities.

The equation tells us that the value of $y$ depends on the value of $x$. If we choose a specific value for $x$, we can calculate the corresponding value of $y$ using the equation. For example, if we set $x$ to 2, we can find $y$ by substituting $x = 2$ into the equation:

Now, let's practice solving problems involving dependent and independent variables.

#### Practice problems on dependent and independent variables

1. Identify the dependent and independent variables in the following equation: $y = 3x - 2$.

2. Solve the equation $2x + 5 = 15$ to find the value of the dependent variable.

3. In a school play, the number of tickets sold depends on the ticket price. Write an equation to represent this relationship, where $y$ represents the number of tickets sold and $x$ represents the ticket price.

Take your time to solve these problems, and remember to identify the dependent and independent variables in each case.

### Section: Analyzing relationships between variables

In the previous section, we learned about dependent and independent variables and how they are used in equations and inequalities. Now, let's take a closer look at analyzing relationships between variables.

#### Understanding relationships between variables

When we have two or more variables in an equation or inequality, we can analyze the relationship between them to gain a deeper understanding of how they interact. By studying these relationships, we can make predictions, solve problems, and draw conclusions.

One common way to analyze relationships between variables is by creating a table of values. This involves choosing different values for one variable and calculating the corresponding values for the other variable. By plotting these values on a graph, we can visualize the relationship between the variables.

Let's go through an example to illustrate this concept:

**Example:**

Suppose we have the equation $y = 2x + 3$. In this equation, $y$ is the dependent variable, and $x$ is the independent variable. To analyze the relationship between $x$ and $y$, we can create a table of values:

| $x$ | $y$ |
|-----|-----|
| 0   | 3   |
| 1   | 5   |
| 2   | 7   |
| 3   | 9   |

By substituting different values for $x$ into the equation, we can calculate the corresponding values for $y$. Plotting these points on a graph, we can see that they form a straight line. This indicates a linear relationship between $x$ and $y$.

Analyzing the relationship between variables can also involve looking for patterns or trends. For example, if we notice that as $x$ increases, $y$ also increases, we can infer that there is a positive relationship between the variables. On the other hand, if $x$ increases while $y$ decreases, we can infer a negative relationship.

Understanding relationships between variables is essential in various areas of mathematics, such as algebra, geometry, and statistics. It allows us to make predictions, solve real-world problems, and analyze data.

In the next section, we will explore different types of relationships between variables and learn how to represent them using graphs and equations.

### Section: Analyzing relationships between variables

Now, let's practice analyzing relationships between variables with some practice problems.

#### Practice problems on analyzing relationships between variables

1. Consider the equation $y = 3x - 2$. Create a table of values for $x$ and $y$ using the following values for $x$: 0, 1, 2, 3, and 4. Plot the points on a graph and describe the relationship between $x$ and $y$.

2. Solve the equation $2x + 5 = 15$ to find the value of $x$. Then, substitute this value into the equation $y = 3x + 2$ to find the corresponding value of $y$. What is the relationship between $x$ and $y$ in this case?

3. Consider the inequality $y > 2x - 1$. Choose three different values for $x$ and determine whether each value satisfies the inequality. Plot the points on a graph and shade the region that satisfies the inequality. What can you conclude about the relationship between $x$ and $y$ based on the graph?

Take your time to solve these practice problems and make sure to show your work. Remember to analyze the relationship between variables and describe any patterns or trends you observe.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamental concepts of equations and inequalities in 6th grade math. We began by understanding what equations are and how they represent a balance between two expressions. We learned how to solve equations by performing the same operation on both sides of the equation, ensuring that the balance is maintained. We also discussed the importance of checking our solutions to ensure their validity.

Next, we delved into inequalities, which are similar to equations but involve comparisons between two expressions. We learned about the different symbols used to represent inequalities and how to interpret them on a number line. We explored solving inequalities by applying the same operations to both sides, while also considering the direction of the inequality.

Throughout this chapter, we practiced solving various types of equations and inequalities, including those with variables on both sides, fractions, and decimals. We also learned how to solve word problems involving equations and inequalities, applying our mathematical knowledge to real-life scenarios.

By mastering equations and inequalities, you have gained a solid foundation in algebraic thinking. These skills will serve as building blocks for more advanced math concepts in the future. Remember to practice regularly and apply what you have learned to solve a wide range of problems.

Congratulations on completing the Equations & Inequalities chapter! You are now ready to tackle more complex mathematical challenges with confidence.
